      RL4CO: AN E XTENSIVE R EINFORCEMENT L EARNING
002   FOR C OMBINATORIAL O PTIMIZATION B ENCHMARK
003
004
005    Anonymous authors
006    Paper under double-blind review
007
008                                                 A BSTRACT
009
010              Deep reinforcement learning (RL) has recently shown significant benefits in solv-
011
                 ing combinatorial optimization (CO) problems, reducing reliance on domain ex-
                 pertise, and improving computational efficiency. However, the field lacks a uni-
012
                 fied benchmark for easy development and standardized comparison of algorithms
013
                 across diverse CO problems. To fill this gap, we introduce RL4CO, a unified and
014              extensive benchmark with in-depth library coverage of 23 state-of-the-art meth-
015              ods and more than 20 CO problems. Built on efficient software libraries and best
016              practices in implementation, RL4CO features modularized implementation and
017              flexible configuration of diverse RL algorithms, neural network architectures, in-
018              ference techniques, and environments. RL4CO allows researchers to seamlessly
019              navigate existing successes and develop their unique designs, facilitating the entire
020              research process by decoupling science from heavy engineering. We also provide
021              extensive benchmark studies to inspire new insights and future work. RL4CO has
022
                 attracted numerous researchers in the community and is open-sourced.1
023
024
      1       I NTRODUCTION
025
026
      Combinatorial optimization (CO) focuses on finding optimal solutions for problems with discrete
      variables and has broad applications, including vehicle routing (Nazari et al., 2018; Kool et al.,
027
      2019a), scheduling (Zhang et al., 2020), and hardware device placement (Kim et al., 2023). Given
028
      that the combinatorial space expands exponentially and exhibits NP-hard characteristics, the op-
029   erations research (OR) community has traditionally tackled these challenges through the devel-
030   opment of mathematical programming algorithms (Gurobi Optimization, 2021) and handcrafted
031   heuristics (Mart et al., 2018). Despite their success, these methods still face significant limitations:
032   mathematical programming struggles with scaling, while handcrafted heuristics require significant
033   domain-specific adjustments for different CO problems.
034
      Recently, to address these limitations, neural combinatorial optimization (NCO) has emerged (Ben-
035   gio et al., 2021b). It employs deep neural networks to automate the problem-solving process and
036   significantly reduces computation demands and domain expertise requirements. Recent NCO works
037   mainly leverage the reinforcement learning (RL) paradigm, making significant strides in improving
038   exploration efficiency (Kwon et al., 2020; Kim et al., 2021), relaxing the needs of obtaining optimal
039   solutions, and extending to various CO tasks (Zhang et al., 2020; Nazari et al., 2018; Kool et al.,
040   2019a; Kim et al., 2023). Although supervised learning methods (Drakulic et al., 2023) are shown
041   to be effective in NCO, they require a large number of high quality solutions, which is unrealistic for
042   large instances or theoretically hard problems. Therefore, this work focuses on the RL paradigm.
043   Despite the growing popularity and advancements in using RL for solving CO problems, there re-
044   mains a lack of a unified benchmark for analyzing past works under consistent implementations and
045   conditions. The absence of a standardized benchmark hinders NCO researchers’ efforts to make
046   impactful advancements and leverage existing successes, as it becomes challenging to determine the
047   superiority of one method over another. Moreover, the significance of NCO lies in its potential for
048   generalizability across multiple problems without extensive problem-specific knowledge. Variations
049
      in implementation can make it difficult for new researchers to engage with the NCO community, and
      inconsistent comparisons obstruct straightforward performance evaluations. These issues pose sig-
050
      nificant challenges and underscore the need for a comprehensive benchmark to streamline research
051
      and foster consistent progress.
052

065
066
067   Contributions. To bridge this gap, we introduce RL4CO, the first comprehensive benchmark with
068   multiple baselines, environments, and boilerplate from the literature, all implemented in a modular,
069   flexible, accelerated, and unified manner. Our aim is to facilitate the entire research process for the
070   NCO community with the following key contributions: 1) Simplifying development through modu-
071   larizing 27 environments and 23 existing baseline models, allowing for flexible and automated com-
072   binations for effortless testing, switching, and achieving state-of-the-art performance; 2) Enhancing
073
      the training and testing efficiency through the customized unified pipeline tailored for the NCO com-
      munity based on advanced libraries such as TorchRL (Bou et al., 2024), PyTorch Lightning (Falcon
074
      and The PyTorch Lightning team, 2019), Hydra (Yadan, 2019), and TensorDict (Moens, 2023); 3)
075
      Standardizing evaluation to ensure fair and comprehensive comparisons, enabling researchers to au-
076   tomatically test a broader range of problems from diverse distributions and gather valuable insights
077   using our testbed. Overall, RL4CO eliminates the need for repetitive heavy engineering in the NCO
078   community and fosters seamless future development by building on existing successes, enabling
079   advanced innovation and progress in the field.
080
081
      2      R ELATED W ORKS
082
083
      Neural Combinatorial Optimization. Neural combinatorial optimization (NCO) utilizes machine
084
      learning techniques to automatically develop novel heuristics for solving NP-hard CO problems.
085   We classify the majority of NCO research from the following perspectives: 1) Learning Paradigms:
086   researchers have employed supervised learning (Vinyals et al., 2015; Hottung et al., 2020; Sun and
087   Yang, 2023; Drakulic et al., 2023; Luo et al., 2024a) to approximate optimal solutions to CO in-
088   stances. Further research leverages reinforcement learning (Bello et al., 2017; Nazari et al., 2018;
089   Kool et al., 2019a; Kwon et al., 2020), and unsupervised learning (Min et al., 2023) to ease the dif-
090   ficulty of obtaining (near-)optimal solutions. 2) Models: various deep learning architectures such as
091   recurrent neural networks (Vinyals et al., 2015; Chen and Tian, 2019; Li et al., 2023), graph neural
092   networks (Joshi et al., 2019; Min et al., 2023), Transformers (Kool et al., 2019a; Kwon et al., 2020),
093
      diffusion models (Sun and Yang, 2023), and GFlowNets Zhang et al. (2023); Kim et al. (2024) have
      been employed. 3) Problems: NCO has demonstrated great success in various problems, including
094
      vehicle routing problems (VRPs) (e.g., traveling salesman problem (TSP) and capacitated VRP),
095
      scheduling problems (e.g., job shop scheduling problems (Zhang et al., 2020)), hardware device
096   placement (Kim et al., 2023), and graph-based CO (Zhang et al., 2023). 4) Heuristic Types: in
097   general, the learned heuristics can be categorized as constructive in an autoregressive (Kool et al.,
098   2019a) or non-autoregressive (Joshi et al., 2019) way, and improvement heuristics that leverage tra-
099   ditional heuristics (Wu et al., 2021; Ma et al., 2024) and meta-heuristics (Song et al., 2020). We refer
100   to Bengio et al. (2021b) for a comprehensive survey. In this paper, we focus on the reinforcement
101   learning paradigm due to its effectiveness and flexibility. Notably, the proposed RL4CO is versatile
102   to support most combinations of models, problems and heuristic types, making it an apt library and
103   benchmark for future research in NCO.
104   Related Benchmark Libraries. Despite the variety of general-purpose RL software libraries
105   (Brockman et al., 2016; Liang et al., 2017; Raffin et al., 2021; Weng et al., 2022) there is a lack
106   of a unified and extensive benchmark for CO problems. Balaji et al. (2019) propose an RL bench-
107   mark for OR that comes only with a PPO baseline (Schulman et al., 2017). Also, Hubbs et al.
      (2020) and Biagioni et al. (2022) implement a collection of OR environments, but do not provide


                                                                    2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
      any baselines to solve them. Wan et al. (2023) propose an RL for OR library that benchmarks the
109   canonical TSP and CVRP environments using different configurations of the attention model (Kool
110   et al., 2019a). Despite having a different focus, we also mention the work of Prouvost et al. (2020)
111   here, who develop an API to use RL for controlling traditional MILP solvers (Linderoth et al., 2010),
112   rather than using RL directly to learn solutions. Besides their narrow scope, a major downside of
113   the above libraries is that they cannot be massively parallelized due to their reliance on the OpenAI
114   Gym API (Brockman et al., 2016), which can only run on CPU. In contrast, RL4CO is based on
115   TorchRL (Bou et al., 2024), a recent official PyTorch (Paszke et al., 2019) library for RL that enables
116   hardware-accelerated execution of both environments and algorithms.
117   In contrast to the works above, Routing Arena (Thyssens et al., 2023) provides multiple neural and
118   classical baselines, but benchmarks these only on the CVRP. The most related recent work is Jumanji
119   (Bonnet et al., 2024), which provides a variety of CO environments written in JAX (Bradbury et al.,
120   2018) that can be hardware-accelerated alongside an actor-critic baseline. While Jumanji is an RL
121   environment suite, RL4CO is a full-stack library that integrates environments, policies, and RL
122   algorithms under a unified framework. As such, baselines in RL4CO are modular and applicable to
123
      all suitable CO problems, whereas in Jumanji, policies are tailored to a specific environment.
124
125
126   3   RL4CO: TAXONOMY
127
128
129   We describe the RL4CO taxonomy, categorizing components into Environments, Policies, and RL
130   Algorithms. Then, we translate the taxonomy to implementation in § 4.
131   Environments. Given a CO problem instance x, we formulate the solution-generating procedure as
132   a Markov Decision Process (MDP) characterized by a tuple (S, A, T , R, γ) as follows. State S is
133   the space of states that represent the given problem x and the current partial solution being updated
134   in the MDP. Action A is the action space, which includes all feasible actions at that can be taken at
135   each step t. State Transition T is the deterministic state transition function st+1 = T (st , at ) that
136   updates a state st to the next state st+1 . Reward R is the reward function R(st , at ) representing
137   the immediate reward received after taking action at in state st . Finally, γ ∈ [0, 1] is a discount
138
      factor that determines the importance of future rewards. Since the state transition is deterministic,
      we represent the solution for a problem x as a sequence of T actions a = (a0 , . . . , aT −1 ). Then,
139                   PT −1
140
      the total return t=0 R(st , at ) translates to the negative cost function of the CO problem.
141   Policies. The policies can be categorized into constructive policies, which generate a solution from
142   scratch, and improvement policies, which refine an existing solution.
143
      Constructive policies. A policy π is used to construct a solution from scratch for a given problem
144   instance x. It can be further categorized into autoregressive (AR) and non-autoregressive (NAR)
145   policies. An AR policy is composed of an encoder f that maps the instance x into an embedding
146   space h = f (x) and a decoder g that iteratively determines a sequence of actions a as follows:
147                                                              −1
                                                                TY
148               at ∼ g(at |at−1 , ..., a0 , st , h), π(a|x) ≜     g(at |at−1 , . . . , a0 , st , h). (1)
149                                                               t=1
150
      A NAR policy encodes a problem x into a heuristic H = f (x) ∈ RN         + , where N is the number of
151
      possible assignments across all decision variables. Each number in H represents an (unnormalized)
152   probability of a particular assignment. To obtain a solution a from H, one can sample a sequence
153   of assignments from H while dynamically masking infeasible assignments to meet problem-specific
154   constraints. It can also guide a search process, e.g., Ant Colony Optimization (Dorigo and Stützle,
155   2019; Ye et al., 2023; Kim et al., 2024), or be incorporated into hybrid frameworks (Ye et al., 2024b).
156   Here, the heuristic helps identify promising transitions and improve the efficiency of finding an
157   optimal or near-optimal solution.
158   Improvement policies. A policy can be used for improving an initial solution a0 = (a00 , . . . , a0T −1 )
159   into another one potentially with higher quality, which can be formulated as follows:
160                                                          YK
161                      ak ∼ g(a0 , h), π(aK |a0 , x) ≜        g(ak |ak−1 , ..., a0 , h),                 (2)
                                                              k=1

170                    Figure 1: Overview of different types of policies and their modularization in RL4CO.
171
172   where ak is the k-th updated solution and K is the budget in terms of the number of improvements.
173
      This process allows continuous refinement over time to improve the quality of the solution.
174   RL Algorithms. The RL objective is to learn a policy π that maximizes the expected cumulative
175   reward (or equivalently minimizes the cost) over the distribution of problem instances:
                                                   "          "T −1                ##
176                                                             X
177                         θ∗ = argmax Ex∼P (x) Eπ(a|x)            γ t R(st , at ) ,           (3)
                                                     θ                                           t=0
178
179   where θ is the set of parameters of π and P (x) is the distribution of problem instances. Eq. (3)
180
      can be solved using algorithms such as variations of REINFORCE (Sutton et al., 1999), Advantage
      Actor-Critic (A2C) methods (Konda and Tsitsiklis, 1999), or Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO)
181
      (Schulman et al., 2017). These algorithms are employed to train the policy network π, by trans-
182
      forming the maximization problem in Eq. (3) into a minimization problem involving a loss function,
183   which is then optimized using gradient descent algorithms. For instance, the REINFORCE loss
184   function gradient is given by:
185                        ∇θ La (θ|x) = Eπ(a|x) [(R(a, x) − b(x))∇θ log π(a|x)] ,                      (4)
186   where b(·) is a baseline function used to stabilize training and reduce gradient variance. We also
187   distinguish between two types of RL (pre)training: 1) inductive and 2) transductive RL. In inductive
188   RL, the focus is on learning patterns from the training dataset to generalize to new instances, thus
189   amortizing the inference procedure. Conversely, transductive RL (or test-time optimization) opti-
190   mizes parameters during testing on target instances. Typically, a policy π is trained using inductive
191   RL, followed by transductive RL for test-time optimization.
192
193   4      RL4CO: L IBRARY S TRUCTURE
194
195   RL4CO is a unified reinforcement learning (RL) for combinatorial optimization (CO) library that
196   aims to provide a modular, flexible, and unified code base for training and evaluating RL for CO
197   methods with extensive benchmarking capabilities on various settings. As shown in Fig. 2, RL4CO
198   decouples the major components of an RL pipeline, prioritizing their reusability in the implementa-
199   tion. Following also the taxonomy of § 3, the main components are: (§ 4.1) Environments, (§ 4.2)
200   Policies, (§ 4.3) RL algorithms, (§ 4.4) Utilities, and (§ 4.5) Baselines Zoo.
201
202                   Env Configs
                                                               reset                                          { action }
203
                                                                                         Policy                                    Env
                                                    { Instance }
                      Policy Configs
204
                                                                                           PyTorch                                 TorchRL
205
                      RL Configs
                        ......
                                                         TensorDict                                           { states }
206
207                            Configs                                                                                           RL Algorithm
208
                         Figure 2: Overview of the RL4CO pipeline: from configurations to training a policy.
209
210
211   4.1      E NVIRONMENTS
212
213   Environment Interface. Environments in RL4CO fully specify the CO problems and their logic in
214   a stateless manner. That is, all static and dynamic information about the environment like the cur-
215   rent state st , actions at , and rewards rt are passed to and retrieved from the environment’s reset
      and step functions through a TensorDict (Moens, 2023). This not only enables modular in-


                                                                                    4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
      teractions between environments and policies, but also allows for seamless integration with various
217   components without the need for environment-specific adaptations. Further, a key advantage com-
218   pared to other libraries is that environments in RL4CO can take batches of instances and process
219   them in parallel on a GPU. Instances x are provided through a modular generator to the envi-
220   ronment, and different generators can be used to generate different data distributions, facilitating the
221   generalization of trained policies.
222
      RL4CO’s environments are based on the RL4COEnvBase class that extends the EnvBase of
223   TorchRL (Bou et al., 2024) with additional features and efficiency improvements. For instance,
224   RL4CO’s step method brings a decrease of up to 50% in latency and halves the memory impact
225   by keeping only required transitions in the stateless TensorDict. Additionally, our environment
226   API contains several functions, such as render and check_solution_validity, helping to
227   analyze generated solutions, select_start_nodes for multi-start methods like POMO (Kwon
228   et al., 2020) and local_search for iterative solution improvement.
229   Problems. We include benchmarking for 27 environments divided into four areas. 1) Routing:
230   Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) (Lawler et al., 1986), Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem
231   (CVRP) (Bodin, 1983), Orienteering Problem (OP) (Laporte and Martello, 1990; Chao et al., 1996),
232   Prize Collecting TSP (PCTSP) (Balas, 1989), Pickup and Delivery Problem (PDP) (Kalantari et al.,
233   1985; Savelsbergh and Sol, 1995) and Multi-Task VRP (MTVRP), which includes 16 problem vari-
234   ants, namely the basic VRPTW, OVRP, VRPB, VRPL and VRPs with the respective constraint com-
235   binations (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou et al., 2024; Berto et al., 2024); 2) Scheduling: Flexible Job Shop
236   Scheduling Problem (FJSSP) (Brandimarte, 1993), Job Shop Scheduling Problem (JSSP) (Rand,
237
      1982) and Flexible Flow Shop Problem (FFSP); 3) Electronic Design Automation: multiple De-
      cap Placement Problem (mDPP) (Kim et al., 2023); 4) Graph: Facility Location Problem (FLP)
238
      (Drezner and Hamacher, 2004) and Max Cover Problem (MCP) (Khuller et al., 1999). A detailed
239
      description of the environment implementations for these problems can be found in Appendix B.
240
241
242   4.2   P OLICIES
243
      Policies in RL4CO are subclasses of PyTorch’s nn.Module and contain the encoding-decoding
244
      logic and neural network parameters θ. Drawing on our taxonomy in § 3, RL4CO pro-
245
      vides different metaclasses like AutoregressivePolicy, NonAutoregressivePolicy,
246   or ImprovementPolicy that the different policies in the RL4CO “zoo” can inherit from.
247   RL4CO modularize components to process environment specific features into the embedding space
248   via parametrized functions. First, node embeddings ϕn : RN ×mn → RN ×h transform mn raw
249   features for the N nodes of problem x from the feature space to the embedding space h. Further,
250   edge embeddings ϕe : RE×me → RE×h transform me edge features of instance x from the fea-
251   ture space to the embedding space h, where E is the number of edges. Lastly, context embeddings
252   ϕc : Rmc → Rh capture contextual information not related to a specific node or edge by transform-
253   ing mc context features from the current decoding step st from the feature space to the embedding
254
      space h. Overall, Fig. 3 illustrates a generic constructive AR policy in RL4CO, where the feature
      embeddings are applied similarly to other types of policies. Feature projections can be automatically
255
      selected by RL4CO at runtime by simply passing the environment to the policy. Additionally, we
256
      allow for granular control of any higher-level policy component , such as encoders and decoders.
257
258
259   4.3   RL A LGORITHMS
260
261
      RL algorithms in RL4CO are used to learn the parameters θ of the Policy by interact-
      ing with the Environment and its problem instances. The parent class of algorithms is the
262
      RL4COLitModule, inheriting from PyTorch Lightning’s pl.LightningModule (Falcon and
263
      The PyTorch Lightning team, 2019). This allows for granular support of various methods includ-
264   ing the [train, val, test]_step, automatic logging with several logging services such as
265   Wandb via log_metrics, automatic optimizer configuration via configure_optimizers
266   and several useful callbacks for RL methods such as on_train_epoch_end. RL algorithms
267   are additionally attached to an RL4COTrainer, a wrapper we made with additional optimizations
268   around pl.Trainer. This module seamlessly supports features of modern training pipelines, in-
269   cluding logging, checkpoint management, mixed-precision training, various hardware acceleration
      supports (e.g., CPU, GPU, TPU, and Apple Silicon), and multi-device distribution (Li et al., 2020b).

280   Figure 3: Overview of modularized RL4CO policies. Any component such as the encoder/decoder structure
281   and feature embeddings can be replaced and thus the model is adaptable to various new environments.
282
283
      For instance, using mixed-precision training significantly decreases training time without sacrificing
284
      much convergence and enables us to leverage recent routines, e.g., FlashAttention (Dao et al., 2022;
285   Dao, 2023), which we investigate in Appendix E.7.2.
286
287
      4.4     U TILITIES
288
289   Configuration Management. Optional, but useful, RL4CO utilizes Hydra (Yadan, 2019), a
290   framework that enables hierarchical configuration management. This not only makes it easy to
291   define complex configurations and manage lots of experiments with different (hyperparameter) set-
292   tings, but also facilitates reproducability of experiments by freezing experimental setups in configu-
293   ration files. We outline the process of configuration management with Hydra in Appendix D.3.1.
294   Decoding Schemes. Decoding schemes define the logic of translating from the model’s unnormal-
295   ized log-probabilities to actions. Specifically, they handle the transformation from the unnormalized
296   log-probabilities to probabilities P (A) by masking infeasible actions and optionally applying tanh
297   clipping (Bello et al., 2017) prior to softmax normalization. Subsequently, different decoding strate-
298   gies can be employed to determine the action based on the probability distribution: 1) Greedy, which
299   selects the action with the highest probability; 2) Sampling, which samples from the masked proba-
300   bility distribution of the policy, where different sampling strategies like softmax temperature scaling
301
      τ , top-k sampling (Kool et al., 2019b), and top-p (or Nucleus) sampling (Holtzman et al., 2019) can
      be used; 3) Multistart, which enforces diverse starting actions as demonstrated in POMO (Kwon
302
      et al., 2020), such as starting from different cities in the TSP; 4) Augmentation, which applies trans-
303
      formations to instances, such as random rotations in Euclidean problems (Kim et al., 2022), to create
304   an augmented set of problems. We describe these strategies in detail in Appendix D.4.
305
306   Documentation, Tutorials, and Testing. We release extensive documentation2 to make RL4CO as
307
      accessible as possible for both newcomers and experts. Furthermore, several tutorials and examples
      are also available under the examples/ folder of our publicly available code3 . We thoroughly
308
      test our library via continuous integration on multiple Python versions and operating systems. The
309
      following code snippet shows minimalistic code that can train a model in a few lines:
310
311
      from rl4co.envs.routing import TSPEnv, TSPGenerator
312   from rl4co.models import AttentionModelPolicy, POMO
313   from rl4co.utils import RL4COTrainer
314   # Instantiate generator and environment
315   generator = TSPGenerator(num_loc=50, loc_distribution="uniform")
      env = TSPEnv(generator)
316
      # Create policy and RL model
317   policy = AttentionModelPolicy(env_name=env.name, num_encoder_layers=6)
318   model = POMO(env, policy, batch_size=64)
319   # Instantiate Trainer and fit
320   trainer = RL4COTrainer(epochs=10, accelerator="gpu", precision="fp16")
      trainer.fit(model)

324
      4.5   BASELINES Z OO
325
326   RL4CO entails a collection of 23 neural solvers for CO from the literature, which are implemented
327   in a modular and flexible way using our policy metaclasses introduced in § 4.2. We organize these
328   baselines into five categories: (non-)autoregressive constructive methods, improvement strategies,
329   general-purpose RL algorithms and transductive RL methods. Table 2 lists all baselines currently
330   available in RL4CO and we refer the reader to Appendix C for further implementation details.
331
                                                  Table 2: RL4CO baselines.
332
333
       Category                   Methods
334
                                  AM (Kool et al., 2019a), Ptr-Net (Vinyals et al., 2015), POMO (Kwon et al., 2020),
335                               HAM (Li et al., 2021), SymNCO (Kim et al., 2022), PolyNet (Hottung et al., 2024),
       Constructive AR Methods
336                               MVMoE (Zhou et al., 2024), L2D (Zhang et al., 2020), HGNN (Song et al., 2022),
                                  MatNet (Kwon et al., 2021), MTPO (Liu et al., 2024a), DevFormer (Kim et al., 2023)
337
       Constructive NAR Methods   DeepACO (Ye et al., 2023), GFACS (Kim et al., 2024), GLOP (Ye et al., 2024b)
338
       Improvement Methods        DACT (Ma et al., 2021), N2S (Ma et al., 2022), NeuOpt (Ma et al., 2024)
339
                                  REINFORCE (Sutton et al., 1999), Advantage Actor-Critic (A2C) (Konda and Tsitsiklis, 1999),
340    RL Algorithms
                                  Proximal Policy Optimization (PPO) (Schulman et al., 2017)
341
       Transductive RL Methods    Active search (Bello et al., 2017), Efficient active search (Hottung et al., 2022)

      5     B ENCHMARKING S TUDY
346
      We perform several benchmarking studies with our unified RL4CO library, with experimental setup
347
      and benchmarking details in Appendix D. Due to the extent of our benchmark, we report highlights
348
      of the results in the following section and refer the reader to Appendix E for additional experiments.
349
350
      5.1   F LEXIBILITY AND M ODULARITY
351
352   Changing policy components. The integration of many state-of-the-art methods in RL4CO
353   from the NCO field in a modular framework makes it easy to implement and improve upon
354   state-of-the-art neural solvers for complex CO problems with only a few lines of code.4
355   We demonstrate this in Table 3 for the

369   5.2   C ONSTRUCTIVE P OLICIES

371   Mind Your Baseline. In on-policy RL, which is often employed in RL4CO due to fast reward
372
      function evaluations, several different REINFORCE baselines have been proposed to improve the
      performance. We benchmark several RL algorithms training constructive policies for routing prob-
373
      lems of node size 50, whose underlying architecture is based on the encoder-decoder Attention
374
      Model (Kool et al., 2019a) and whose main difference lies in how the REINFORCE baseline is
375   calculated (we additionally train the AM with PPO as further reference). For a fair comparison,

432
      5.3   C OMBINING C ONSTRUCTION AND I MPROVEMENT: B EST OF B OTH W ORLDS ?
433
434   While constructive policies can build solutions in seconds, their performance is often lim-
435   ited, even with advanced decoding schemes such as sampling or augmentations.                            On
436   the other hand, improvement methods are more suitable for larger computing budgets.
437   We benchmark models on TSP with 50 nodes: the
438   AR constructive method POMO (Kwon et al., 2020)               0.5
439   and the improvement methods DACT (Ma et al.,                                  POMO (sampling)
                                                                    0.4             DACT (from random init)
440   2021) and NeuOpt (Ma et al., 2024). In the original
                                                                                    DACT (from POMO init)
      implementation, DACT and NeuOpt started from a


                                                                Gap (%)
441                                                                 0.3             NeuOpt (from random init)
      solution constructed randomly. To further demon-                              NeuOpt (from POMO init)
442
      strate the flexibility of RL4CO, we show that boot-           0.2
443
      strapping improvement methods with constructive
444   ones enhance convergence speed. Fig. 5 shows                  0.1
445   that bootstrapping with a pre-trained POMO policy
446   significantly enhances the convergence speed. To              0.0
                                                                        0      50     100        150         200
447   further investigate the performance, we report the
                                                                                    Time (s)
448   Primal Integral (PI) (Berthold, 2013; Vidal, 2022;
449   Thyssens et al., 2023), which evaluates the evolution Figure 5: Bootstrapping improvement with con-
450   of solution quality over time. Improvement methods structive methods.
451   alone, such as DACT and NeuOpt, achieve 2.99 and
452
      2.26 respectively, while sampling from POMO achieves 0.08. This shows that the “area under the
      curve” can be better even if the final solution is worse for constructive methods. Bootstrapping with
453
      POMO then improves DACT and NeuOpt to 0.08 and 0.04 respectively, showing the benefits of
454
      modularity and hybridization of different components.
455
456
457   6     D ISCUSSION
458
459   Limitations and Future Directions While RL4CO is an efficient and modular library specialized
460   in CO problems, it might not be suitable for any other task due to a number of area-specific opti-
461   mizations, and we do not expect it to seamlessly integrate with, for instance, OpenAI Gym wrappers
462   without some modifications. Another limitation of the library is its scope so far, namely RL. Even-
463   tually, creating a new library to support supervised methods as a comprehensive "AI4CO" codebase
464   could benefit the whole NCO community. We additionally identify in Foundation Models for CO
465   and related scalable architectures a promising area of future research to overcome generalization
466   issues across tasks and distributions, for which we provided some early clues in Appendix E.8.
467
468   Long-term Plans RL4CO is an active library that has already garnered much attention from the
469   community, with over 400 stars on GitHub. We thank contributors in the community who have
470   helped us build RL4CO. Our long-term plan is to become the go-to RL for CO benchmark library.
471   For this purpose, we created a community Slack workspace (link available upon acceptance) that
472   has attracted more than 200 researchers. We are committed to helping resolve issues and questions
473
      from the community and actively engaged in discussion. It is our hope that our work will ultimately
      benefit the NCO field with new ideas and collaborations. More available in Appendix A.
474
475
476   7     C ONCLUSION
477
478
      This paper introduces RL4CO, a modular, flexible, and unified Reinforcement Learning (RL) for
479
      Combinatorial Optimization (CO) benchmark. We provide a comprehensive taxonomy from envi-
480   ronments to policies and RL algorithms that translate from theory to practice to software level. Our
481   benchmark library aims to fill the gap in unifying implementations in RL for CO by utilizing sev-
482   eral best practices with the goal of providing researchers and practitioners with a flexible starting
483   point for NCO research. We provide several experimental results with insights and discussions that
484   can help identify promising research directions. We hope that our open-source library will provide
485   a solid starting point for NCO researchers to explore new avenues and drive advancements. We
      warmly welcome researchers and practitioners to actively participate and contribute to RL4CO.

       A.1     W HY C HOOSING THE RL4CO L IBRARY ?
1083
1084   RL4CO is a unified and extensive benchmark for the RL-for-CO research domain, designed to be
1085   accessible and valuable to researchers and practitioners across all levels of expertise.
1086
1087
1088
1089
1090
1091
1092                                     Figure 6: RL4CO benchmark logo.
1093
1094
1095
       Availability and Future Support RL4CO can be installed through PyPI. We adhere to continu-
       ous integration, deployment, and testing to ensure reproducibility and accessibility.
1096
1097
       Open License We adopt the open MIT license for all content contained in RL4CO. We ascribe
1098
       to the principles of libre software5 . Most reimplementations are from original authors and are re-
1099   licensed under the MIT license. Data and baseline-specific licenses are reported in Appendix A.3.

1101
1102
1103
1104

1107
1108   Open Community Through our journey, we started the AI4CO community6 , which is a non-
1109   profit, cross-institution, inclusive, and open research community. AI4CO originally started out as a
1110
       Slack channel for discussing the RL4CO but evolved into a broader-visioned and inclusive space to
       communicate with other researchers about general NCO. We warmly invite all interested people to
1111
       join us.

                                          Figure 8: AI4CO community logo.

1120
1121   A.2     O N THE C HOICE OF THE S OFTWARE
1122
1123   During the development of RL4CO, we wanted to make it as simple as possible to integrate repro-
1124   ducible and standardized code adhering to the latest guidelines. As a main template for our codebase,
1125   we use Lightning-Hydra-Template 7 which we believe is a solid starting point for reproducible deep
1126   learning. We further discuss framework choices below.
1127
1128
       PyTorch PyTorch (Paszke et al., 2019) is a popular open-source deep-learning framework that has
       gained significant traction in the research community. We chose PyTorch as the primary framework
1129
       for RL4CO due to its intuitive API, dynamic computational graphs, strong community support,
1130
       and seamless integration with the Python ecosystem. These features make PyTorch well-suited for
1131
           5
1132         https://www.gnu.org/philosophy/free-sw.en.html
           6
1133         Community Github link available upon acceptance
           7
             https://github.com/ashleve/lightning-hydra-template


                                                          21
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1134
       rapid prototyping and experimentation, which are essential in research settings. Moreover, most
1135   of the existing research in NCO has been implemented. It is currently being implemented using
1136   PyTorch, making it not only easier to build upon and compare with previous work but also easier for
1137   newcomers and experienced researchers.
1138
1139   TorchRL and TensorDict One of the software hindrances in RL is the bottleneck between CPU
1140   and GPU communication, majorly due to CPU-based operating environments. For this reason, we
1141   did not opt for OpenAI Gym (Brockman et al., 2016) since, although it includes some level of par-
1142   allelization, this does not happen on GPU and would thus greatly hinder performance. Kool et al.
1143   (2019a) creates ad-hoc environments in PyTorch to handle batched data efficiently. However, it
1144   could be cumbersome to integrate into standardized routines that include step and reset func-
1145
       tions. As we searched for a better alternative, we found that TorchRL library (Bou et al., 2024),
       an official PyTorch project that allows for efficient batched implementations on (multiple) GPUs as
1146
       well as functions akin to OpenAI Gym. We also employ the TensorDict (Bou et al., 2024) to handle
1147
       tensors efficiently on multiple keys (i.e. in CVRP, we can directly operate transforms on multiple
1148   keys as locations, capacities, and more). This makes our environments compatible with the models
1149   in TorchRL, which we believe could further spread interest in the CO area.
1150
1151   PyTorch Lightning PyTorch Lightning (Falcon and The PyTorch Lightning team, 2019) is a use-
1152   ful tool for abstracting away the boilerplate code, allowing researchers and practitioners to focus
1153   more on the core ideas and innovations. It features a standardized training loop and an extensive
1154   set of pre-built components, including automated checkpointing, distributed training, and logging.
1155   PyTorch Lightning accelerates development time and facilitates scalability. We employ PyTorch
1156   Lightning in RL4CO to integrate with the PyTorch ecosystem - which includes TorchRL- enabling
1157
       us to leverage the rich set of tools and libraries available.
1158
       Hydra Hydra (Yadan, 2019) is a powerful open-source framework for managing complex con-
1159
       figurations in machine-learning models and other software. Hydra facilitates creating hierarchical
1160
       configurations, making it easy to manage even very large and intricate configurations. Moreover, it
1161   integrates with command-line interfaces, allowing the execution of different configurations directly
1162   from the command line, thereby enhancing reproducibility. We found Hydra to be effective when
1163   dealing with multiple experiments since configurations are saved both locally, as yaml files, and
1164   can be uploaded to monitoring software as Wandb 8 (or to any of the monitoring software supported
1165   by PyTorch Lightning).
1166

1194
       B.1     ROUTING
1195
1196   Routing problems are perhaps the most known class of CO problems. They are problems of great
1197   practical importance, not only for logistics, where they are more commonly framed, but also for
1198   industry, engineering, science, and medicine. The typical objective of routing problems is to mini-
1199   mize the total length of the paths needed to visit some (or all) the nodes in a graph. In the following
1200   section, we present each of these variants with details of their implementations.
1201
1202   Common instance generation details Following the standard protocol of NCO for routing, we
1203
       randomly sample node coordinates from the 2D unit square (i.e., [0, 1]2 ). To ensure reproducibility
       in our experiments, we use specific random seeds for generating validation and testing instances. For
1204
       the 10,000 validation instances, we use a random seed of 4321. For the 10,000 testing instances, we
1205
       use a random seed of 1234. All protocols, including seed selection, align with the practices outlined
1206   by Kool et al. (2019a).
1207
1208
       B.1.1    T RAVELING S ALESMAN P ROBLEM (TSP)
1209
1210   The Traveling Salesman Problem (TSP) is a fundamental routing problem that aims to find the
1211   Hamiltonian cycle of minimum length. While the original TSP formulation employs mixed-integer
1212   linear programming (MILP), in the NCO community, the solution-finding process of TSP is dif-
1213   ferently formulated for constructive and improvement methods. For constructive methods, the TSP
1214
       solution is generated by autoregressive solution decoding (i.e., the construction process) in line with
       Kool et al. (2019a). In each step of node selection, we preclude the selection of nodes already picked
1215
       in previous rounds. This procedure ensures the feasibility of constructed solutions and also allows
1216
       for the potential construction of an optimal solution for any TSP instance. For improvement meth-
1217   ods, it starts with an initial solution and iteratively searches for an optimal one using local search.
1218   In each step, the solution is locally adjusted based on a specified local search operator. We support
1219   two representative operators for TSP variants, including the 2-opt in line with Ma et al. (2021) and
1220   the flexible k-opt in line with Ma et al. (2024). The former selects two nodes in the current solution
1221   and reverses the solution segment between them to perform a 2-opt exchange. The latter selects k
1222   nodes so that a k-opt is performed. Both methods ensure the feasibility of the solutions by masking
1223   invalid actions. The best solution after a set number of iterations is the final output.
1224
                                Optimal (or BKS)                                   AM 50                                       POMO 50                                    SymNCO 50                                     AM-XL 50
1225              1200                                         1200                                         1200                                         1200                                         1200

                  1000                                         1000                                         1000                                         1000                                         1000
1226
                  800                                          800                                          800                                          800                                          800

                  600                                          600                                          600                                          600                                          600

1228              400                                          400                                          400                                          400                                          400

1229              200                                          200                                          200                                          200                                          200

1234
1235
       B.1.2    C APACITATED V EHICLE ROUTING P ROBLEM (CVRP)
1236   The Capacitated Vehicle Routing Problem (CVRP) is a popular extension of TSP, applicable to a
1237   variety of real-world logistics/routing problems (e.g., delivery services). In CVRP, each node has its
1238   own demand, and the vehicle visiting them has a specific capacity and always leaves from a special
1239   node called “depot”. The vehicle can visit new nodes while their demand fits in its residual capacity
1240   (i.e. the total capacity decreased by the sum of the demands visited in the current path). When no
1241   nodes can be added to the path, the vehicle returns to the depot, and its full capacity is restored.
       Then, it embarks on another tour. The process is repeated until all nodes have been visited. By


                                                                                                                             23
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1242
       applying a similar logic to that of the TSP environment, we can reformulate CVRP as a sequential
1243   node selection problem, taking into account demands and capacity.
1244
1245                               Optimal (or BKS)                        AM 50                           POMO 50                          SymNCO 50                        AM-XL 50

1246                 80                                    80                                80                               80                               80


1247                 60                                    60                                60                               60                               60


1248                 40                                    40                                40                               40                               40


1249                 20                                    20                                20                               20                               20


1250                  0
                                               D
                                                           0
                                                                                   D
                                                                                             0
                                                                                                                     D
                                                                                                                              0
                                                                                                                                                     D
                                                                                                                                                               0
                                                                                                                                                                                        D

                          0   20      40       60     80        0   20    40       60   80        0   20    40      60   80        0   20    40      60   80        0   20    40      60    80
                                     Cost: 1167                          Cost: 1200                        Cost: 1181                       Cost: 1196                       Cost: 1176

          Figure 10: Sample CVRP tours on CVRPLib’s A-n54-k7 instance with different autoregressive models.
1253
1254
1255   Additional generation details To generate the demand, we randomly sample integers between 1
1256   and 10. Without loss of generality, we fix the capacity of the vehicle at 1.0. Then, we normalize
1257   the demands by multiplying them by a constant that varies according to the size of the CVRP. The
1258
       specific constant can be found in our implementation.
1259
1260
       B.1.3      O RIENTEERING P ROBLEM (OP)
1261   The Orienteering Problem (OP) is a variant of the TSP. In the OP, each node is assigned a prize. The
1262   objective of the OP is to find a tour, starting and ending at the depot, that maximizes the total prize
1263   collected from visited nodes, while abiding by a maximum tour length constraint. The OP can be
1264   framed as a sequential decision-making problem by enforcing the “return to depot” action when no
1265   nodes are visitable due to the maximal tour length constraint.
1266
1267   Additional generation details To generate the prize, we use the prize distribution proposed in
1268   Fischetti et al. (1998), particularly the distribution that allocates larger prizes to nodes further from
1269
       the depot.
1270
1271
       B.1.4      P RIZE C OLLECTING TSP (PCTSP)
1272   In the Prize Collecting TSP (PCTSP), each node is assigned both a prize and a penalty. The objec-
1273   tive is to accumulate a minimum total prize while minimizing the combined length of the tour and
1274   the penalties for unvisited nodes. By making a minor adjustment to the PCTSP, it can model dif-
1275   ferent subproblems that arise when using the Branch-Price-and-Cut algorithms for solving routing
1276   problems.
1277
1278   B.1.5      P ICKUP AND D ELIVERY P ROBLEM (PDP)
1279
       The Pickup and Delivery Problem (PDP) is an extension of TSP in the literature Helsgaun (2017);
1280
       Ma et al. (2022).9 In PDP, a pickup node has its own designated delivery node. The delivery node
1281   can be visited only when its paired pickup node has already been visited. We call this constraint
1282   precedence constraint. The objective of the PDP is to find a complete tour with a minimal tour
1283   length while starting from the depot node and satisfying the precedence constraints. We assume that
1284   stacking is allowed, meaning that the traveling agent can visit multiple pickups prior to visiting the
1285   paired deliveries. For constructive methods, the PDP solution construction is similar to that of TSP
1286   but must obey precedence constraints. For improvement methods, we consider the ruin and repair
1287   local search operator presented by Ma et al. (2021). In each step, a pair of pickup and delivery nodes
1288   are removed from the current solution and then reinserted back into the solution with potentially
1289
       better positions. Invalid actions that violate precedence constraints are masked out to ensure the
       feasibility of PDP solutions.
1290
1291
       Additional generation details To generate the positions of the depot, pickups, and deliveries, we
1292
       sample the node coordinates from the 2D unit square. The first N/2 generated nodes are pickups,
1293   and the remaining N/2 are their respective deliveries. The pickups and deliveries are paired. For a
1294   pickup node i, its respective delivery is i + N/2 (excluding the depot index).
1295
          9
              PDP is also called PDTSP (pickup and delivery TSP).


                                                                                                           24
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       Figure 11: Different VRP attributes. Open routes (O) and duration limits (L) are global attributes, whereas time
1307   windows (TW), capacitated vehicles for linehaul demands (C) and backhaul demands (B) are node attributes.
1308   Attributes may be combined in different ways to define VRP variants.

1310
1311   B.1.6    M ULTI -TASK VRP (MTVRP)
1312
1313
       This environment introduces the 16 VRP variants in Liu et al. (2024a); Zhou et al. (2024) with
       additional enhancements, such as support for any number of variants in the same batch, as done in
1314
       Berto et al. (2024). The base logic is the same as CVRP: each node has a demand, and the vehicle has
1315
       a specific capacity by which it can deliver to nodes and return to the depot to replenish its capacity,
1316   with the goal of minimizing the total tour distance. We report each modular constraint definition in
1317   the following paragraphs according to Berto et al. (2024); Wouda et al. (2024). Table 8 reports the
1318   list of all variants and Fig. 11 illustrates the meaning of each MTVRP component.
1319
1320
                                  Capacity          Open Route               Backhaul          Duration Limit        Time Windows
1321            VRP Variant
                                    (C)                (O)                     (B)                  (L)                  (TW)
1322
                CVRP                 ✓
1323
                OVRP                 ✓                       ✓
1324            VRPB                 ✓                                            ✓
1325            VRPL                 ✓                                                                ✓
1326            VRPTW                ✓                                                                                    ✓
1327            OVRPTW               ✓                       ✓                                                            ✓
                OVRPB                ✓                       ✓                    ✓
1328
                OVRPL                ✓                       ✓                                        ✓
1329            VRPBL                ✓                                            ✓                   ✓
1330            VRPBTW               ✓                                            ✓                                       ✓
1331            VRPLTW               ✓                                                                ✓                   ✓
1332            OVRPBL               ✓                       ✓                    ✓                   ✓
                OVRPBTW              ✓                       ✓                    ✓                                       ✓
1333
                OVRPLTW              ✓                       ✓                                        ✓                   ✓
1334            VRPBLTW              ✓                                            ✓                   ✓                   ✓
1335            OVRPBLTW             ✓                       ✓                    ✓                   ✓                   ✓
1336
1337
       Table 8: The 16 VRP variants that are modeled by the MTVRP environment. All variants include the base
1338   Capacity (C). The k = 4 features O, B, L, and TW can be combined into any subset, including the empty set
1339   and itself (i.e., a power set) with 2k = 16 possible combinations.
1340
       (C) Demand and Vehicle Capacity [q ∈ [0, Q]]: Every node i, except the depot, has a demand qi that
1341
       must be satisfied by the vehicle with a uniform capacity of Q > 0. The sum of the demands served
1342
       by a vehicle in the same path must not exceed its capacity Q at any point along its route.
1343
1344   (O) Open Routes [o ∈ {0, 1}]: With open routes, the distance between the last node and the depot
1345   is not counted in the total path length. This represents the scenarios where vehicles are not required
1346
       to return to the depot after serving all assigned customers. Open routes are commonly found in
       scenarios involving third-party drivers, who are typically compensated only for the deliveries they
1347
       complete, without the need to return to the depot (Li et al., 2007).
1348
1349   (B) Backhauls [p ∈ [0, Q]]: Backhauls extend the concept of demand to include both delivery and
       pickup requests, thus increasing vehicle utilization and leading to savings. Nodes are categorized as


                                                                             25
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1350
       either linehaul or backhaul nodes.10 Linehaul nodes require delivery of demand qi from the depot
1351   to the node i (similar to CVRP), while backhaul nodes require a pickup of an amount pi to be trans-
1352   ported from the node back to the depot. A vehicle can serve both linehaul and backhaul customers in
1353   a single route, but all linehaul customers must be served before any backhaul customers. A typical
1354   example of a backhaul problem is a laundry service for hotels that has to deliver clean towels and
1355   pick up dirty ones, in which the precedence constraint of linehaul nodes is important due to possible
1356   contamination (Çatay, 2009).
1357   (L) Duration Limits [l ∈ [0, L]]: Imposes a limit L on the total travel duration (or distance) of
1358   each vehicle route, ensuring a fair distribution of workload among different paths. This limit is
1359   consistently applied to all routes in the problem.
1360
       (TW) Time Windows [e, s, l ∈ [0, T ]3 ]: Each node i, except for the depot, has an associated time
1361
       window [ei , li ], which specifies the earliest and latest times at which it can be visited. When visiting
1362
       node i, the vehicle must wait for a time si before leaving. The vehicle must arrive at customer i
1363   before the end of its time window li , but if they arrive before the start of the time window ei , they
1364   must wait at the customer’s location until the time window begins before starting the service. When
1365   the vehicle returns to the depot, the time is reset to 0.
1366
1367   Additional generation details We introduce the data generation details as follows:
1368
       Locations: We generate n + 1 locations randomly with xi and yi ∼ U (0, 1), ∀i ∈ {0, . . . , n}, where
1369
       [x0 , y0 ] represents the depot and [xi , yi ], i ∈ {1, . . . , n} are the other n nodes.
1370
1371   Capacity: The capacity C of the vehicle is determined based on the following calculation:
                                           1000 n−1000
1372                              30 +   5  + 33.3            if 1000 < n
1373                         C = 30 + n5                       if 20 < n ≤ 1000 .
                                  
1374                                 30                        otherwise
1375
1376   Open route: the open route is an instance-wise flag: when set to 1, the route is open, when 0 is
1377
       closed. We sample the flag from a uniform distribution with the same probability of the route being
       open or closed.
1378
1379   Linehaul and Backhaul demands: We generate demands according to the following schema:
1380
1381         1. Generate linehaul demands qi ∈ {0, . . . , Q} for all nodes i ∈ {i, . . . , n}. These are needed
1382
                for both backhaul and linehaul scenarios.
1383         2. Generate backhaul demands pi ∈ {0, . . . , Q} for all nodes i ∈ {i, . . . , n}.
1384         3. For each node i ∈ {i, . . . , n}, there is a probability of 0.2 that it is assigned a backhaul
1385            demand, otherwise, its backhaul demand is set to be 0.
1386
1387   Note that even in a backhaul setting, usually not all nodes are backhaul nodes, i.e., we need to
1388   consider both linehaul and backhaul demands in backhaul problem settings. All demands, both
1389
       linehauls and backhauls, are scaled to [0, 1] through division by the vehicle capacity.
1390   Duration limits: Each route is assigned a fixed duration limit L with a default value of 3. We check
1391   that 2 ∗ d0i < L to make sure there is a feasible route for any customer.
1392
       Time Windows: We generate the time windows for each node i ∈ {1, . . . , n} according to the
1393   following steps:
1394
1395         1. Generate service time si ∈ [0.15, 0.18].
1396
             2. Generate time window length ti ∈ [0.18, 0.2].
1397
1398
             3. Calculate distance d0i from node to depot.
1399         4. Calculate the upper bound for the start time hi = tmaxd−s
                                                                       0i
                                                                          i −ti
                                                                                − 1, where tmax is the
1400            maximum time with a default value of 4.6.
1401      10
             Note that another name of this problem, as adopted in LKH3 (Helsgaun, 2017), is VRP with Pickup and
1402   Deliveries (VRPPD). However, we align with PyVRP (Wouda et al., 2024) and do not use this name to prevent
1403   confusion with the one-to-one PDP, as we described before, where there is strict precedence between each pair
       of pickup and delivery.


                                                            26
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1404
             5. Calculate the start time as ei = (1 + (hi − 1) · ui ) · d0i with ui ∼ U (0, 1).
1405
1406         6. Calculate the end time as li = ei + ti .
1407
1408   Classical solvers We employ the SotA HGS implementation in PyVRP (Wouda et al., 2024) and
1409
       OR-Tools (Perron and Furnon, 2023). We make these solvers conveniently available through the
       solve API of the environment.
1410
1411
1412
       B.2     S CHEDULING
1413
       Scheduling problems are a fundamental class of problems in operations research and industrial en-
1414   gineering, where the objective is to optimize the allocation of resources over time. These problems
1415   are critical in various industries, such as manufacturing, computer science, and project manage-
1416   ment. Currently, RL4CO implements three central scheduling problems, namely the flexible flow
1417   shop (FFSP), the job shop (JSSP), and the flexible job shop problem (FJSSP). Each of these prob-
1418   lems has unique characteristics and complexities that need to be translated into the environment
1419   classes that we will describe hereafter.
1420
1421   B.2.1      J OB S HOP S CHEDULING P ROBLEM (JSSP)
1422
       The job shop scheduling problem is a well-known combinatorial optimization problem. It is widely
1423
       used in the operations research community as well as many industries, such as manufacturing and
1424
       transportation. In the JSSP, a set of jobs J must be processed by a set of machines M . Each job
1425   Ji ∈ J consists of a set of ni operations Oi = {oij }nj=1
                                                               i
                                                                  which must be processed one after another
1426   in a given order. The goal of the JSSP is to construct a valid schedule that adheres to the precedence
1427   order of the operations and minimizes the makespan, i.e., the time until the last job is finished. One
1428   example of such a schedule is shown in Fig. 12.
1429
1430                                                                                                      Gantt Chart
1431      Machine 5 41          36             22   49             50        8         30                   9                         44             45                  46
1432
1433                                                                                                                                                                               Job 0
          Machine 4        31             28             7          23                32             14         15     3    16   10             26             35                  Job 1
1434                                                                                                                                                                               Job 2
                                                                                                                                                                                   Job 3
1435                      27         42             1              37 29                    2                          33                  4                   5                   Job 4
          Machine 3                                                                                                                                                                Job 5
1436                                                                                                                                                                               Job 6
                                                                                                                                                                                   Job 7
1437      Machine 2       18    6    48                  19                      38             43                               21                       17                       Job 8
                                                                                                                                                                                   Job 9
1438
1439      Machine 1       47              12                            13             51       20         5239        24   25    40            34                  11
1440
                      0                                       20                                     40                                    60                                 80
1441                                                                                                            Time

1442
1443                                                                     Figure 12: Example Schedule for the JSSP
1444
1445   We formulate the JSSP as a sequential decision problem following the implementation of Tassel
1446   et al. (2021). Here, the environment iterates through distinct time steps t = 1, . . . , T . At each time
1447
       step, the agent decides for each machine whether and which job to process next until all machines
       are busy or all jobs are being processed. In this case, the environment transitions to the next time
1448
       step at which a machine becomes idle.
1449
1450
       Instance Generation We follow the instance generation method described by Zhang et al. (2020),
1451
       which assumes that each job has exactly one operation per machine, i.e. ni = |M |. Further,
1452   processing times for all operations are sampled iid. from a uniform distribution, with parameters
1453   specified in Table 9.
1454
1455   B.2.2      F LEXIBLE J OB S HOP S CHEDULING P ROBLEM (FJSSP)
1456
1457   The flexible job shop scheduling problem is very similar to the JSSP. However, while in the classical
       JSSP, each operation oij ∈ O has a specified machine and processing time pij , the flexible job shop


                                                                                                                27
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1458
       scheduling problem (FJSSP) relaxes this assumption by allowing each operation to be processed by
1459   multiple eligible machines Mk ⊆ M , potentially with different processing times pijk associated
1460   with the respective operation-machine pair. As a consequence, the agent does not only need to
1461   decide which job to process next, but also on which machine it should be processed.
1462
1463
       Instance Generation We follow the instance generation method described by Song et al. (2022),
1464   who sample ni operations for each job Ji from a uniform distribution. Further, an average processing
1465   time p̄ij is drawn for each operation oij ∈ O, and the actual processing time per eligible operation-
1466   machine pair is subsequently sampled from U(0.8 · p̄ij , 1.2 · p̄ij ). The parameters used for instance
1467   generation can be found in Table 9.
1468
1469                                   Table 9: Instance generation parameters
1470
1471                                  JSSP                                              FJSSP
1472                 6×6       10 × 10    15 × 15   20 × 20         10 × 5       20 × 5     15 × 10    20 × 10
1473     |J|           6          10         15        20             10           20          15         20
        |M |           6          10         15        20              5            5          10         10
1474
          ni           6          10         15        20          U(4, 6)       U(4, 6)    U(8, 12)   U(8, 12)
1475     p̄ij       U(1, 99)   U(1, 99) U(1, 99)    U(1, 99)       U(1, 20)      U(1, 20) U(1, 20)     U(1, 20)
1476    |Mi |          1           1          1         1          U(1, 5)       U(1, 5)    U(1, 10)   U(1, 10)
1477
1478
1479
       B.2.3     F LEXIBLE F LOW S HOP P ROBLEM (FFSP)
1480
1481   The flexible flow shop problem (FFSP) is a complex and widely studied optimization problem in
1482   production scheduling. It involves N jobs to be processed in S stages, each containing multiple
1483   machines (M > 1). Each job must pass through the stages in a specified order, but within each
1484   stage, it can be processed by any available machine. A critical constraint is that no machine can
1485   process more than one job at a time. The objective is to find an optimal schedule that minimizes the
1486   total time required to complete all jobs. We formulate the FFSP as a sequential decision process,
1487
       where at each time step t = 0, 1, ... and for each idle machine, the agent must decide whether
       and which job to schedule. If all machines are busy or all jobs are currently being processed, the
1488
       environment moves to the next time step t + 1, and the process repeats until all jobs for each stage
1489
       have been scheduled.
1490
1491
1492
       Instance Generation We follow the data generation process described by Kwon et al. (2021),
       who sample processing times for each job-machine pair and for every stage independently from a
1493
       discrete uniform distribution.
1494
1495
1496   B.3      E LECTRONIC D ESIGN AUTOMATION
1497
1498   c. This involves solving complex problems that can be either continuous, such as cell placement
1499
       (Hou et al., 2024), or combinatorial, like decap placement (Kim et al., 2023). RL4CO integrates CO
       problems in EDA as benchmarking environments.
1500
1501
1502   B.3.1     D ECAP P LACEMENT P ROBLEM (DPP)
1503
       The decap placement problem (DPP) is an electronic design automation problem (EDA) in which
1504
       the goal is to maximize the performance with a limited number of the decoupling capacitor (decap)
1505
       placements on a hardware board characterized by asymmetric properties, measured via a probing
1506   port. The decaps cannot be placed on the location of the probing port or in keep-out regions (which
1507   represent other hardware components) as shown in Fig. 13. The optimal placement of a given num-
1508   ber of decaps can significantly impact electrical performance, specifically in terms of power integrity
1509   (PI) optimization. PI optimization is crucial in modern chip design, including AI processors, espe-
1510   cially with the preference for 3D stacking memory systems like high bandwidth memory (HBM)
1511   (Hwang et al., 2021). For comprehensive details, we follow the configuration guidelines provided
       in (Kim et al., 2023).


                                                         28
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

       GA has shown promise as a method for addressing the decap placement problem (DPP).

1534

1536   the 100 instances.
