1538   B.3.2     M ULTI -P ORT D ECAP P LACEMENT P ROBLEM ( M DPP)
1539
1540   We further consider a more complex and realistic version compared to Kim et al. (2023). The multi-
1541   port decap placement problem (mDPP) is a generalization of DPP from Appendix B.3.1 in which
1542   measurements from multiple probing ports are performed. The objective function can be either the
1543   mean of the reward from the probing ports: 1) (Maxsum): the objective is to maximize the average
1544
       PI among multiple probing ports and 2) (Maxmin): maximize the minimum PI between them.
1545
1546   Instance generation details The generation details are the same as DPP, except for the probing
1547
       port. A number of probing ports between 2 and 5 is sampled from a uniform distribution, and
       probing ports are randomly placed on the board, just like the other components.
1548
1549
1550   B.4     G RAPH
1551
       Many CO problems can be (re-)formulated on graphs (Khalil et al., 2017). In typical CO problems
1552
       on graphs, actions are defined on nodes/edges, while problem variables and constraints are incor-
1553   porated in graph topology and node/edge attributes (e.g., weights). The graph-based formulation
1554   gives us concise and systematic representations of CO problems. Moreover, existing traditional and
1555   machine-learning algorithms for graphs are off-the-shelf tools.
1556
1557   B.4.1     FACILITY L OCATION P ROBLEM (FLP)
1558
1559   The optimal usage of limited resources is an important problem to consider in many different fields
1560   and has various forms. One specific form of such a problem can be formulated as the facility location
1561   problem (FLP), where one aims to choose a given number of locations among given candidates, and
1562
       the objective is to minimize the overall cost of service (e.g., the sum of the distance from the users
       to the nearest facility) (Drezner and Hamacher, 2004).
1563
1564   Many real-world problems can be abstracted as instances of FLP. For example, franchise brands may
1565   need to determine where to open new retail stores to maximize accessibility and profitability (Shan
       et al., 2019); governments may need to consider the placement of public facilities (e.g., hospitals


                                                                29
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1566
       and schools) to maximize the convenience for citizens to use them (Marianov et al., 2002); energy
1567   companies may need to determine the best locations for power centers (e.g., power plants and wind
1568   farms) to minimize transmission losses (Lotfi et al., 2018).
1569
1570   Formal definition We consider the following specific form of the facility location problem (FLP)
1571   used in existing NCO literature (Wang et al., 2022; Bu et al., 2024): (1) given a group of n lo-
1572   cations x1 , x2 , . . . , xn ∈ Rd in a d-dimensional space (usually d = 2 or 3) and k < n, (2) we
1573   aim to choose k locations xi1 , xi2 , . . . , xik among the given n locations as the locations of fa-
1574   cilities,
             Pn(3) to minimize the sum of the distance from all the n locations to the nearest facility,
1575   i.e., j=1 minkt=1 dist(xj , xit ). We specially consider the Euclidean distance, i.e., dist(xi , xj ) =
1576   ∥xi − xj ∥2 .
1577
1578   Instance generation details The locations are (d = 2)-dimensional generated i.i.d. at random.
1579   For each location, each coordinate is sampled i.i.d. uniformly at random between 0 and 1. Each
1580   instance contains n = 100 locations, and k = 10 locations are to be chosen.
1581
1582   Classical solvers We apply two MIP solvers, Gurobi (Gurobi Optimization, 2021) and
1583   SCIP (Bestuzheva et al., 2021), to obtain the optimal solutions.
1584
1585   B.4.2    M AXIMUM C OVERAGE P ROBLEM (MCP)
1586
1587   In many real-world scenarios, one needs to allocate limited resources to achieve maximum coverage,
1588   which is a fundamental concern across various domains. One specific formulation is called the
1589
       maximum coverage problem (MCP), where the goal is to select a subset of sets from a given family
       of sets to maximize the coverage, i.e., the (weighted) size of the union of the selected sets (Khuller
1590
       et al., 1999).
1591
1592   As a mathematical abstraction, the MCP can be used to represent many real-world problems. For
1593   example, radio frequency identification (RFID) system engineers may need to set RFID readers in
1594   an optimal way to ensure the maximum coverage of RFID tags (Ali et al., 2011); marketers may
1595
       need to choose proper forms of advertisement to reach the maximum number of customers (Sun
       et al., 2018); in security applications (e.g., deploying security cameras), one may need to select the
1596
       optimal deployment to maximize the coverage of the protected area (Murray et al., 2007).
1597
1598
       Formal definition We consider the following specific form of the maximum coverage problem
1599
       (MCP) used in existing NCO literature (Wang et al., 2022; Bu et al., 2024): (1) given m items
1600
       (WLOG, [m] := {1, 2, 3, . . . , m}), where each item t has weight wt , and a family of n sets
1601   S1 , S2 , . . . , Sn ⊆ [m] for some positive integer m and k < n, (2) we aim to choose k sets
1602   Si1 , Si2 , . . . , Sik among the given n sets, (3) to maximize the total weighted coverage
                                                                                               P of the k cho-
1603   sen sets, which is the sum of the weights of items contained in any chosen set, i.e., t∈Sk Sij wt .
                                                                                                   j=1
1604
1605
       Instance generation details First, m = 200 items are generated, and the item weights are gener-
1606
       ated i.i.d., where each weight is a random integer sampled between 1 and 10 (inclusive) uniformly at
1607   random. Then, n = 100 sets are generated i.i.d., where for each set, we first sample its size between
1608   5 and 15 uniformly at random and then choose that number of items uniformly at random. After
1609   generation, k = 10 locations are to be chosen.
1610
1611   Classical solvers We apply two MIP solvers, Gurobi (Gurobi Optimization, 2021) and
1612   SCIP (Bestuzheva et al., 2021), to obtain the optimal solutions.
1613
1614   B.5     A DDITIONAL E NVIRONMENTS AND B EYOND
1615
1616   We also include in the library additional environments that have been implemented but not fully
1617   benchmarked in this paper yet, such as the ATSP, mTSP, Skill-VRP, SMTWTP, and SPCTSP, to
1618   name a few. We did not count these in the total environment count (hence the “conservative” esti-
1619   mate). Moreover, several projects, among which co-authors of this paper, have adapted several new
       environments to their own tasks, which may be included in the future.


                                                         30
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1620
       Although RL4CO already contains several environments, we acknowledge that the library can be
1621   further extended within new directions, which we briefly describe. One such direction is multi-
1622   objective combinatorial optimization (Lin et al., 2022; Chen et al., 2024), which is a recently trend-
1623   ing research topic of practical importance. Moreover, providing modular reward evaluators to op-
1624   timize different objectives (for instance, min-max, tardiness) is another avenue of research that we
1625   recommend exploring (Park et al., 2023b). Of practical importance is also non-euclidean routing,
1626   which so far has received comparatively less attention in this field but is practically important (i.e.,
1627   DIMACS challenge11 ). Finally, multi-agent CO (Falkner and Schmidt-Thieme, 2020; Tang et al.,
1628   2024a;b; Bettini et al., 2023) is another interesting area of research, which recent approaches model
1629
       as a sequential decision-making process (Son et al., 2024; Zheng et al., 2024).
1630   Implementing new environments is relatively easy: we created a notebook under the examples/
1631   folder demonstrating how one can implement a custom environment from the base logic to a fully
1632   functioning model. We expect to host an even wider variety of environments in the future, thanks to
1633   the community, and invite contributors to help us in our journey.
1634
1635   C        BASELINES
1636
1637
       This section provides an overview of the key components and methods implemented in RL4CO that
1638   can be used as baselines for comparative evaluation. The term “baselines” broadly refers to both the
1639   RL algorithms that define the learning objectives and update rules, as well as the policy architectures
1640   that parameterize the agent’s behavior in the environment, given that several papers introduce a mix
1641   of RL training schemes and policy improvements. We categorize baselines into:
1642
1643             1. General-purpose RL algorithms (C.1)
1644             2. Constructive autoregressive (AR) methods (C.2)
1645
1646
                 3. Constructive non-autoregressive (NAR) methods (C.3)
1647             4. Improvement methods (C.4)
1648             5. Active search methods (C.5)
1649
1650
       C.1       G ENERAL - PURPOSE RL A LGORITHMS
1651
1652   In the following descriptions of RL algorithms, we use the notations of a full problem instance x
1653   and a complete solution a for simplicity. However, note that these algorithms are also applicable to
1654   the usual notion of the sum of rewards over partial states st and actions at .
1655
1656   C.1.1       REINFORCE (S UTTON ET AL ., 1999)
1657
1658   REINFORCE (also known as policy gradients in the literature) is an online RL algorithm whose loss
1659
       function gradient is given by:
1660
                             ∇θ La (θ|x) = Eπ(a|x) [(R(a, x) − b(x))∇θ log π(a|x)] ,                     (5)
1661   where b(·) is a baseline function used to stabilize training and reduce gradient variance. The choice
1662
       of b(·) can greatly influence the final performance.
1663
1664   C.1.2       A DVANTAGE ACTOR -C RITIC (A2C) (KONDA AND T SITSIKLIS , 1999)
1665
       A2C is an algorithm that can be used to solve the RL objective in Eq. (3). It consists of an actor (pol-
1666   icy network) and a critic (value function estimator). The actor is trained to maximize the expected
1667   cumulative reward by following the policy gradient, while the critic is trained to estimate the value
1668   function. The advantage function, computed as the difference between the reward R(a, x) and the
1669   value function V (x), is used to weight the policy gradient update for the actor. This can be seen
1670   as a modification of the REINFORCE gradient, where the baseline b(x) is replaced by the value
1671   function V (x):
1672                       ∇θ La (θ|x) = Eπ(a|x) [(R(a, x) − V (x))∇θ log π(a|x)] .                         (6)
1673
           11
                http://dimacs.rutgers.edu/programs/challenge/vrp/


                                                         31
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1674
       The critic is updated by minimizing the mean-squared error between the estimated value function
1675   and the target value, which is the reward for the given problem instance x:
1676                                    Lc = Ex∼P (x) (R(a, x) − V (x))2 .                         (7)
1677   By using the advantage function, A2C reduces the variance of the policy gradient and stabilizes
1678   training compared to the standard REINFORCE algorithm.
1679
1680   C.1.3    P ROXIMAL P OLICY O PTIMIZATION (PPO) (S CHULMAN ET AL ., 2017)
1681
1682   PPO is another algorithm that can be used to solve the RL objective in Eq. (3). It is an on-policy
1683   algorithm that aims to improve the stability of policy gradient methods by limiting the magnitude
1684   of policy updates. To this end, PPO introduces a surrogate objective function that constrains the
1685   probability ratio between the target policy πθ that is optimized and a reference policy πθold , which
1686
       is periodically updated. This clipping mechanism prevents drastic changes to the target policy,
       ensuring more reliable and stable learning. Formally, the PPO objective function is given by:
1687                               h                      πθ (a|x) πθ
1688         LCLIP (θ) = Ex∼P (x) Ea∼πθold (a|x) min(                  A old (x, a),
1689
                                                         πθold (a|x)
1690                                                             πθ (a|x)                                 i
                                                         clip(              , 1 − ϵ, 1 + ϵ)Aπθold (x, a)) , (8)
1691                                                            πθold (a|x)
1692   where θold represents the parameters of the reference policy, typically a periodically created copy of
1693   the parameters θ of the target policy. Further, Aπθold (x, a) is the advantage function estimated using
1694   the reference policy, and ϵ is a hyperparameter that controls the clipping range, typically set to a
1695
       small value like 0.2.
1696   The advantage function in PPO is estimated using a learned value function Vϕ (x), where ϕ repre-
1697   sents the parameters of the value function. The advantage is computed as:
1698                                    Aπθold (x, a) = R(a, x) − Vϕ (x).                             (9)
1699   The value function is learned by minimizing the mean-squared error between the estimated value
1700   and the actual return:
1701                                LV (ϕ) = Ex∼P (x) (R(a, x) − Vϕ (x))2 .                          (10)
1702   An optimization step in PPO updates both, the parameters θ of the target policy and the parameters
1703
       ϕ of the value function by combining LCLIP and LV (ϕ) in a single loss LPPO = LCLIP + βLV (ϕ),
       where β is a hyperparameter Schulman et al. (2017).
1704
1705
1706   C.2     C ONSTRUCTIVE AUTOREGRESSIVE (AR)
1707
       C.2.1    ATTENTION M ODEL (AM) (KOOL ET AL ., 2019 A )
1708
1709   The Attention Model (AM) from Kool et al. (2019a) is an encoder-decoder architecture based on the
1710   self-attention mechanism Vaswani et al. (2017) that is at the heart of several state-of-the-art NCO
1711   methods, including RL-based ones (Kwon et al., 2020; Kim et al., 2022; Hottung et al., 2024) as well
1712   as (self-)supervised ones (Drakulic et al., 2023; Luo et al., 2024a;b). In the original AM, only node
1713   features are considered: with abuse of notation from Fig. 3, we consider the InitEmbedding as
1714   the node embedding, and split the context embedding into a ContextEmbedding which updates
1715
       the current query and DynamicEmbedding that updates the current cached keys and values.
1716
1717   Multi-Head Attention Before delving into the encoder and decoder structures, we briefly intro-
1718
       duce the notion of Multi-Head Attention (MHA) from Vaswani et al. (2017), since it is used across
       several NCO methods. MHA allows the model to jointly attend to information from different rep-
1719
       resentation subspaces at different positions, enabling it to capture various relationships between the
1720
       input elements. Importantly, it is flexible in handling a variable number of elements.
1721
1722   In the MHA operation, the input sequences Q (queries), K (keys), and V (values) are linearly
1723   projected to H different subspaces using learned matrices WiQ , WiK , and WiV , respectively, where
1724   H is the number of attention heads:
1725                                              Qi = QWiQ                                           (11)
1726                                                Ki = KWiK                                             (12)
1727
                                                    Vi = V WiV                                            (13)


                                                         32
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1728
       for i = 1, . . . , H.
1729
1730   The attention weights are computed as the scaled dot-product between the queries and keys, followed
1731   by a softmax operation:
                                                           Qi KiT
1732                                       Ai = Softmax √         +M                                   (14)
1733                                                          dk
1734   where dk is the dimension of the keys, used as a scaling factor to prevent the dot-products from
1735
       getting too large, and M is an optional mask matrix that can be used to prevent attention to certain
       positions (e.g. infeasible actions in a CO problem).
1736
1737   The output of each attention head is computed as the weighted sum of the values, using the attention
1738   weights:
1739                                                Zi = Ai Vi                                         (15)
1740   Finally, the outputs of all attention heads are concatenated and linearly projected using a learned
1741
       matrix W O to obtain the final output of the MHA operation:
1742                                MHA(Q, K, V ) = Concat(Z1 , . . . , ZH )W O                        (16)
1743   This multi-head attention mechanism allows the model to learn different attention patterns and cap-
1744
       ture various dependencies between the input elements, enhancing the representational power of the
       model. The queries, keys, and values can come from the same input sequence (self-attention, i.e.
1745
       Q = K = V ) or from different sequences (cross-attention), depending on the application. While the
1746
       attention operation is at the core of much of the current SotA deep learning (Touvron et al., 2023),
1747   this scales as O(L)2 where L is the sequence length, such as the number of nodes in a TSP. Thus,
1748   an efficient implementation such as FlashAttention (Dao et al., 2022; Dao, 2023) is important, as
1749   shown in Appendix E.7.2.
1750
1751   Encoder The encoder’s primary task is to encode input x into a hidden embedding h. The struc-
1752   ture of fθ comprises two trainable modules: the InitEmbedding and encoder blocks. The
1753   InitEmbedding module typically transforms problem features into the latent space and problem-
1754   specific compared to the encoder blocks, which often involve plain multi-head attention (MHA):
1755                          h = fθ (x) ≜ EncoderBlocks(InitEmbedding(x))                         (17)
1756
       Each encoder block in the AM is composed of an Attention Layer, similar to Vaswani et al. (2017).
1757
       Each layer ℓ is composed of multi-head attention (MHA) for message passing and a Multi-Layer
1758   Perceptron (MLP, also known as feed-forward network (FFN)), with skip-connections and normal-
1759   ization (Norm):
1760
                                    ĥ = Norm h(ℓ−1) + MHA(h(ℓ−1) , h(ℓ−1) , h(ℓ−1) )                                              (18)
1761

1762                              h(ℓ) = Norm ĥ + MLP(ĥ)                                                                         (19)
1763
1764   with ℓ = [1, . . . , N ] where N is the number of encoding layers and h = InitEmbedding(x). In      0

1765   the encoder side, we have Q = K = V = h(ℓ−1) ), hence self-attention.
                                                                                                 dh
1766   The original implementation of the AM uses N = 3 layers H = 8 heads of dimension dk = M      =
1767   16, an MLP with one hidden layer of dimension 512 with a ReLU activation function, and a Batch
1768   Normalization (Ioffe and Szegedy, 2015) as normalization.
1769
1770                                Encoder                                               Decoder
                                 Initial   Encoder       Hidden States
1771                                                                          Context Embedding
                               Embedding    Layers

       Decoder The decoder gθ autoregressively constructs the solution based on the encoder output h
1783   and the state at current step t, st . The solution decoding involves iterative steps until a complete
1784   solution is constructed: at each step, starting from the current node’s i query qti
1785                                      qti = ContextEmbedding(h, st ),                               (20)
1786
1787
                                        hct = MHA(qti , Ktg , Vtg , Mt ),                                (21)
1788                                           Vtp hct
                                         z= √                                                             (22)
1789                                              dk
                                                                                                g    g   p
1790   where Mt is the set of feasible actions (i.e. the action_mask), projections Kt , Vt , Vt =
1791   Wkg h, Wvg h, Wvp h can either be precomputed once as cache or updated via a dynamic embedding
1792   Ktg , Vtg , Vtp = DynamicEmbedding(Wkg h, Wvg h, Wvp h, st , h, x),, depending on the problem.
1793   We note that Eq. (22) is usually referred to as the pointer mechanism (in the codebase, we refer
1794   to Eq. (21) and Eq. (22) as the PointerAttention). Finally, logits z (unnormalized output of
1795
       policy π) are transformed into a probability distribution over the action space:
1796
                                             p = Softmax (C · tanh(z))                                    (23)
1797
       where logits z for infeasible actions can be set to −∞ to avoid choosing them; and the C value
       (called tanh clipping, usually set to 10) serves in improving the exploration (Bello et al., 2017). We
1798
       note that Eq. (23) can also include additional operations such as temperature scaling, top-k, and
1799
       top-p filtering.
1800
1801
1802   Baseline Kool et al. (2019a) additionally introduces the rollout baseline b for Eq. (5). At the end of
1803
       each epoch, a greedy rollout of a baseline policy πBL is executed for each of the sampled instances
       x, whose values become baselines for REINFORCE. The algorithm compares the current training
1804
       policy with a saved baseline policy (similar to the DQN target network (Mnih et al., 2015)) at the
1805
       end of every epoch, and replace the parameters of πBL with the current trained π if the improvement
1806   is significant with a paired t-test of (i.e., 5% in the original paper).

       C.2.2   P TR -N ET (V INYALS ET AL ., 2015)
1809
1810   The original Pointer Network (Ptr-Net) is introduced in Vinyals et al. (2015) and further refined to be
1811   trained with RL in (Bello et al., 2017). The base architecture predates the AM (Kool et al., 2019a):
1812   an attention mechanism is employed to select outputs of variable length, thus “pointing” at them.
1813   The baseline architecture additionally uses an LSTM (Hochreiter and Schmidhuber, 1997), which
1814   in practice has less expressivity than full-fledged attention.
1815
1816   C.2.3   POMO (K WON ET AL ., 2020)
1817
1818   POMO introduces the shared baseline to lower the REINFORCE variance. The key idea is that one
1819   can sample rollouts when decoding by forcing diverse starting nodes, which is a powerful inductive
1820   bias for certain problems, such as the TSP, in which multiple optimal initial starting points exist.
1821
       The baseline bshared is the average of all rollouts:
                                                              N
1822                                                        1 X
                                            bshared (s) =       R(aj , x)                             (24)
1823                                                      N j=1
1824
       where N is the number of sampled trajectories (typically set as the number of nodes).
1825
1826
1827   C.2.4   S YM NCO (K IM ET AL ., 2022)
1828
       SymNCO considers the symmetric nature of combinatorial problems and solutions. There are two
1829
       major symmetries in combinatorial optimization: 1) Problem symmetries: The representation of
1830   the input 2D coordinates should have equivalent optimal solution sets and 2) Solution symmetries:
1831   Multiple permutations can represent an identical cyclic line graph. To reflect this symmetric nature,
1832   SymNCO augments the AM architecture by incorporating an auxiliary invariant representation loss
1833   function to ensure input 2D symmetries. Additionally, SymNCO employs a shared baseline as
1834   Eq. (24) similar to POMO but samples rollouts from both different symmetric problem inputs and
1835   solutions together. The implementation is not vastly different from AM and POMO; the primary
       addition is the symmetric-aware augmentation functions.


                                                         34
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1836
       C.2.5   P OLY N ET (H OTTUNG ET AL ., 2024)
1837
1838   The PolyNet method proposed by Hottung et al. (2024) enables the learning of a set of complemen-
1839   tary solution strategies within a single model. This facilitates the easy sampling of diverse solutions
1840   at test time, resulting in improved exploration of the search space and, consequently, enhanced over-
1841   all performance. Unlike many other approaches, PolyNet does not artificially increase exploration
1842   by forcing diverse starting actions, as initially proposed by Kwon et al. (2020). Instead, PolyNet uti-
1843
       lizes its inherent diversity mechanism, based on its novel architecture and the Poppy loss (Grinsztajn
       et al., 2023; Chalumeau et al., 2024):
1844
                              ∇θ L = Eπ(a∗ |x) [(R(a∗ , x) − b◦ (x))∇θ log πθ (a∗ |x)] ,                  (25)
1845
       to facilitate exploration during the search process, where a∗ is the best solution of K PolyNet sam-
1846
       ples and b◦ (x)) is the average reward of the K samples. This can improve performance for problems
1847
       in which the first action greatly influences the performance.
1848
1849
       C.2.6   HAM (L I ET AL ., 2021)
1850
1851   The Heterogeneous Attention Model (HAM) (Li et al., 2021) is a model specialized for Pickup
1852   and Delivery problems (PDP, Appendix B.1.5), characterized by hard one-to-one precedence con-
1853   straints. To differentiate between pickup and delivery pairs, it introduces ad hoc encoder blocks with
1854   a specialized attention mechanism that can differentiate between pickup and delivery pairs.
1855
1856   C.2.7   MTPOMO (L IU ET AL ., 2024 A )
1857
1858
       The MTPOMO developed by Liu et al. (2024a) proposes to adopt a unified model to learn across
       various VRP variants. It is motivated by the fact that the diverse VRPs are different combinations of
1859
       several shared underlying attributes. By training on a limited number of VRPs with basic attributes,
1860
       the model is capable of generalizing to a vast array of VRP variants, each representing different
1861   combinations of these attributes. This approach extends POMO (Kwon et al., 2020) by incorporating
1862   an attribute composition block, facilitating learning across different problems. The cross-problem
1863   learning demonstrates promising zero-shot generation performance on unseen VRPs and benefits
1864   out-of-distribution performance.
1865
1866   C.2.8   MVM O E (Z HOU ET AL ., 2024)
1867
1868   The MVMoE architecture proposed by Zhou et al. (2024) incorporates mixture-of-experts
1869   (MoEs) (Jacobs et al., 1991; Jordan and Jacobs, 1994; Shazeer et al., 2017) into attention-based
1870
       model (e.g., POMO (Kwon et al., 2020)), such that the model capacity can be greatly enhanced with-
       out a proportional increase in computation. For the encoder part, MVMoE replaces a feed-forward
1871
       network (FFN) with an MoE layer, which typically consists of 1) m experts {E1 , E2 , . . . , Em },
1872
       each of which is also an FFN with independent trainable parameters, and 2) a gating network G
1873   parameterized by WG , which decides how the inputs are distributed to experts. Given a single input
1874   x, G(x) and Ej (x) denote the output of the gating network (i.e., an m-dimensional vector), and the
1875   output of the jth expert, respectively. The output of an MoE layer is calculated as:
1876                                                   Xm

1877                                        MoE(x) =       G(x)j Ej (x).                              (26)
1878                                                   j=1

1879   The gating algorithm follows the node-level input-choice gating proposed by Shazeer et al. (2017),
1880   which leverages a sparse gating network: G(x) = Softmax(TopK(x · WG )). In this way, only
1881
       k experts with partial model parameters are activated, hence saving the computation. For the de-
       coder part, MVMoE replaces the final linear layer of MHA with an MoE layer, including m linear
1882
       layers and a gating network G. To balance the empirical performance and computational com-
1883
       plexity, a hierarchical gating mechanism is further proposed to utilize MoEs during decoding effi-
1884   ciently. In this case, the MoE layer in the decoder includes two gating networks {G, G′ }, m experts
1885   {E1 , E2 , . . . , Em }, and a dense layer D. Given a batch of inputs X, the hierarchical gating routes
1886   them in two stages. In the first stage, G′ decides to distribute inputs X to either the sparse or dense
1887   layer. In the second stage, if X is routed to the sparse layer, the gating network G is activated to
1888   route nodes to experts on the node level by using the default gating algorithms, i.e., the input-choice
1889   gating. Otherwise, X is routed to the denseP    layer D and transformed into D(X). In summary, the
                                                         m
       hierarchical gating learns to output G′ (X)0 j=1 G(X)j Ej (X) or G′ (X)1 D(X). Empirically, hi-


                                                         35
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1890
       erarchical gating has been found to be more efficient, albeit with a slight sacrifice in in-distribution
1891   performance, while demonstrating superiority with out-of-distribution data.
1892
1893   C.2.9    L2D (Z HANG ET AL ., 2020)
1894
1895   Learning to Dispatch (L2D) proposed by Zhang et al. (2020) is a DRL method to solve the JSSP.
1896   It comprises of the usual encoder-decoder structure, where a graph convolution network (GCN) is
1897   employed to extract hidden representations from the JSSP instance. To this end, L2D formulates the
1898
       JSSP as a disjunctive graph, with nodes reflecting the operations of the problem instance. Nodes of
       operations that belong to the same job are connected via directed arcs, specifying their precedence
1899
       relation. Moreover, operations to be processed on the same machine are connected using undirected
1900
       arcs. Using the resulting neighborhood N of the nodes, the GCN performs massage passing be-
1901   tween adjacent operations to construct their hidden representations. Formally, let h0 be the initial
1902   embeddings of operations O and Ã the adjacency matrix with added self-loops of operations, then
1903   a graph convolutional layer can be described as follows:
1904
1905                                                   1        1

1906                                   h(l+1) = σ D̃ − 2 ÃD̃ − 2 h(l) W (l)
1907
       Here, h(l) are the operation embeddings at layer l, W (l) is a trainable weight matrix at layer l, and
1908
1909
       σ(·) is an activation function such as ReLU. Further, D̃ is the diagonal degree matrix of Ã, ensuring
       appropriate scaling of the features.
1910
1911   Given the operation embeddings, the decoder of L2D first extracts for each job the embedding of
1912   the operation that needs to be scheduled next and then feeds them to an MLP f : RJ×d → RJ×1 to
1913   obtain logits for each job j ∈ (1, ..., J). In contrast to Kool et al. (2019a) for example, who encode
1914   the CO problem once and then generate actions autoregressively using only the decoder, Zhang et al.
1915
       (2020) use the GCN encoder after each step to generate new hidden representations that reflect the
       current state of the problem.
1916
1917
       C.2.10    HGNN (S ONG ET AL ., 2022)
1918
1919   The heterogeneous graph neural network (HGNN) is a neural network architecture proposed by Song
1920   et al. (2022) to solve the FJSSP. Similar to L2D, HGNN considers an FJSSP instance as a graph.
1921   However, instead of treating an FJSSP instance as a disjunctive graph, Song et al. (2022) formulate it
1922   as heterogeneous graph with operations and machines posing different node types. Again, operations
1923   are connected to each other via directed arcs that specify the precedence relation. Machines are only
1924
       connected to operations that they are able to process, and the edge weights indicate the respective
       processing times. To encode the graph, HGNN first projects operations O ∈ x and machines M ∈ x
1925
       into a mutual embedding space Rd using type-specific transformations W O and W M , respectively.
1926
       Given the initial hidden representations h0i and h0k for operations oi ∈ O and machines mk ∈ M ,
1927   respectively, as well as edge embeddings hik , an HGNN layer conducts weighted message passing
1928   between operations and machines using the processing times of operation-machine pairs:
1929
                                                    X
                                           hl+1
                                             i   =       ϵi hlj , where                                 (27)
1930
                                                     j∈Ni
1931
1932                                        ϵij = Softmax(a⊤ [hlj ||hij ]).                                 (28)
                                                    j∈Ni
1933
1934   Since operations in the FJSSP can be processed by multiple machines, the decoder must specify
1935
       not only which job to process next but also on which machine the operation of the selected job
       should be executed. To this end, Song et al. (2022) concatenates the hidden representations of every
1936
       operation with the embeddings of every machine. The resulting embeddings are fed to an MLP
1937
       f : RJ×M ×2d → RJ×M ×1 , which generates the sampling probabilities for the respective action.
1938
1939   C.2.11    M AT N ET (K WON ET AL ., 2021)
1940
1941   The MatNet architecture proposed by Kwon et al. (2021) adjusts the attention model Kool et al.
1942   (2019a) so that it is applicable to bipartite graphs with node types I and J as well as a weight
1943   matrix E ∈ R|I|×|J | corresponding to the edges connecting nodes from the two sets. The novelty
       of this architecture is that instead of using self-attention as in the attention model, MatNet uses cross-

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

1945   scores with the weight matrix E. Formally, let Z be the set of all nodes i ∈ I ∪ J , Zϕi the subset
1946

1947

       C.2.12       D EV F ORMER (K IM ET AL ., 2023)
1970
1971   We employ online RL variants of DevFormer (Kim et al., 2023) (DF), an Attention-Model (Kool
1972   et al., 2019a) variant specifically designed for autoregressive construction of DPP solutions from
1973   Appendix B.3.1. We note that the DF training scheme was initially designed for offline training;
1974   however, in this study, we benchmark DF as a sample-efficient online reinforcement learning ap-
1975   proach. We benchmark the DF version for RL with the same node and context embedding structure
1976   as the original in Kim et al. (2023). We modify the embeddings in the mDPP environment (Ap-
1977
       pendix B.3.2) version to include the location of multiple probing ports. Min-max and min-sum
       mDPP versions utilize the same embeddings and are trained separately.

1979
       C.3      C ONSTRUCTIVE N ON -AUTOREGRESSIVE (NAR)
1980
1981   C.3.1      D EEPACO (Y E ET AL ., 2023)
1982
1983   Ant Colony Optimization (ACO) is an evolutionary algorithm that has been successfully applied to
1984   various COPs. Traditionally, customizing ACO for a specific problem requires the expert design
1985   of knowledge-driven heuristics. However, this routine of algorithm customization exhibits certain
1986
       deficiencies: 1) it requires extra effort and makes ACO less flexible; 2) the effectiveness of the
       heuristic measure heavily relies on expert knowledge and manual tuning; and 3) designing a heuristic
1987
       measure for less-studied problems can be particularly challenging, given the paucity of available
1988
       expert knowledge.
1989
1990   DeepACO is designed to automatically strengthen the heuristic measures of existing ACO algo-
1991   rithms and dispense with laborious manual design in future ACO applications. DeepACO consists
1992
       of two stages: 1) training a neural model to map a COP instance to its heuristic measures, and 2) in-
       corporating the learned heuristic measures into ACO to bias solution constructions and local search.
1993
       During the training phase, DeepACO parameterizes the heuristic space with a graph neural network
1994
       (GNN) (Joshi et al., 2019). It trains the GNN across COP instances with REINFORCE, towards
1995   minimizing the expected objective value of both constructed solutions and solutions refined by local
1996   search. During the inference phase, DeepACO utilizes the well-trained GNN to generate heuristic
1997
         12
              For succinctness, note that we omit head and layer enumeration.


                                                              37
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1998
       measures for ACO. Optionally, DeepACO interleaves local search with neural-guided perturbation
1999   to refine the constructed solutions. For more details, please refer to (Ye et al., 2023).
2000
2001   DeepACO is the first NAR model implemented in RL4CO, laying the foundation for other NAR
2002
       models later integrated into RL4CO. DeepACO offers a versatile methodological framework that
       allows for further algorithmic enhancements in neural architecture, training paradigms, decoding
2003
       strategies, and problem-specific adaptations. Notable improvements over DeepACO are introduced
2004
       by GFACS (Kim et al., 2024).
2005
2006
2007
       C.3.2    GFACS (K IM ET AL ., 2024)
2008
       While DeepACO (Ye et al., 2023) provides promising results and opens new doors for pretraining
2009   heuristic measures for the ACO algorithm using deep learning, their method is sub-optimal for two
2010   major reasons. Firstly, they utilized policy gradient reinforcement learning (RL), which is an on-
2011   policy method that cannot leverage powerful off-policy techniques such as local search. Secondly,
2012   their method cannot effectively capture the multi-modality of heuristic distribution because the RL
2013   method cannot accurately model multi-modal probabilistic distributions considering the symmetric
2014   nature of combinatorial space, where multiple trajectories can lead to identical solutions.
2015   The methodology of GFACS shares a very similar structure with DeepACO. The key difference
2016   lies in the learning procedure; GFACS employs generative flow networks (GFlowNets) (Bengio
2017   et al., 2021a; 2023) for learning the heuristic matrix. Additionally, they leverage effective off-policy
2018   exploration methods using local search. The inference procedure with the learned heuristic matrix
2019   remains exactly the same. With the RL4CO modular implementation, both DeepACO and GFACS
2020   can run similarly and be comparable at the modular level, allowing future researchers to improve
2021   certain modules of training or inference.
2022
2023   C.3.3    GLOP (Y E ET AL ., 2024 B )
2024
2025   Most NCO methods struggle with real-time scaling-up performance; they are unable to solve routing
2026
       problems involving thousands or tens of thousands of nodes in seconds, falling short of the needs
       of modern industries. GLOP (Global and Local Optimization Policies) is proposed to address this
2027
       challenge. It partitions a large routing problem into sub-TSPs and further partitions potentially large
2028
       (sub-)TSPs into small Shortest Hamiltonian Path Problems (SHPPs). It is the first hybrid method to
2029   integrate NAR policies for coarse-grained problem partitions and AR policies for fine-grained route
2030   constructions, leveraging the scalability of the former and the meticulousness of the latter.
2031
2032
       1) AR (Sub-)TSP Solver. The (Sub-)TSP Solver in GLOP initializes TSP tours using a Random
2033
       Insertion heuristic, which greedily inserts nodes to minimize cost. These tours are then improved
2034
       through a process of decomposition and reconstruction. Specifically, the solver decomposes a com-
2035   plete tour into several subtours, which are treated as instances of the Shortest Hamiltonian Path
2036   Problem (SHPP). Each subtour is solved using an AR local policy referred to as a “reviser”. These
2037   revisers are applied in rounds called “revisions” to enhance the initial tour iteratively. The subtours
2038   are normalized and optionally rotated to improve the model’s performance. After solving the SHPP
2039   instances, the subtours are reassembled into an improved complete tour. This method allows for
2040   efficient and parallelizable improvements on large-scale TSPs.
2041
2042   2) NAR General Routing Solver. The general routing solver in GLOP additionally implements
2043   an NAR global policy that either partitions all nodes into multiple sub-TSPs (e.g., for CVRP) or
2044   subsets all nodes to form a sub-TSP (e.g., for PCTSP). The NAR global policy is parameterized by a
2045   graph neural network (GNN) that processes sparsified input graphs and outputs a partition heatmap.
2046   GLOP clusters or subsets nodes by sequentially sampling nodes based on the partition heatmap
2047   while adhering to problem-specific constraints. The sub-TSPs are then solved by the (Sub-)TSP
2048
       solver. The global policy is trained using REINFORCE to output partitions that could lead to the
       best-performing final solutions after solving sub-TSPs.
2049
2050   GLOP is integrated into RL4CO as the first hybrid method that combines NAR and AR policies,
2051   indicating the versatility of RL4CO in accommodating various methodological paradigms. It is
       promising to further investigate the emerging possibilities that arise when viewing AR and NAR


                                                         38
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2052
       methods from a unified perspective and combining them synergistically. RL4CO provides a flexible
2053   and extensible platform for exploring such hybridization in future research.
2054
2055
       C.4     I MPROVEMENT METHODS
2056
2057   Improvement methods leverage RL to train a policy that iteratively performs rewriting exchanges on
2058   the current solution, aiming to generate a new solution with potentially lower costs. As in construc-
2059   tive methods, the policy of improvement methods is also based on the encoder-decoder structure.
2060
2061   C.4.1    DACT (M A ET AL ., 2021)
2062
2063
       Improvement methods typically take node features and solution features (positional information of
       nodes in the current solution) as key inputs. Encoding VRP solutions involves processing com-
2064
       plex relationships between Node Feature Embeddings (NFEs) and Positional Feature Embeddings
2065
       (PFEs). However, directly adopting the original Transformer to add the two types of embeddings, as
2066   done by Wu et al. (2021), can cause mixed attention score correlations and impairing performance.
2067   To address this, the Dual-Aspect Collaborative Transformer (DACT) proposes DAC-Att, which pro-
2068   cesses NFEs and PFEs separately and employs cross-aspect referential attention to understand the
2069   consistencies and differences between the two embedding aspects. This approach avoids mixed
2070   correlations and allows detailed modeling of hidden patterns. Another key issue is the Positional
2071   Encoding (PE) method. While the original Transformer’s PE works well for linear sequences, it
2072   may not suit the cyclic nature of VRP solutions. To address this, DACT proposes Cyclic Positional
2073   Encoding (CPE), inspired by cyclic Gray codes, which generates cyclic real-valued coding vectors to
2074
       capture the topological structure of VRP solutions and improve generalization. Additionally, DACT
       redesigns the RL algorithm for improvement methods, introducing a Proximal Policy Optimization
2075
       with Curriculum Learning (PPO-CL) algorithm to improve training stability and efficiency.
2076
2077   In RL4CO, DACT is implemented and modularized so that other methods can easily reuse com-
2078   ponents like CPE encoding and the PPO-CL algorithm. It also reuses common parts (such as node
2079   embedding initialization, decoding functions, etc) from the implementation of constructive methods,
2080
       indicating the flexibility of the RL4CO framework.
2081
2082
       C.4.2    N2S (M A ET AL ., 2022)
2083   The Neural Neighborhood Search (N2S) method extends the capabilities of improvement methods
2084   to pickup and delivery problems (PDP). Expanding on the DACT approach, N2S leverages a tai-
2085   lored MDP formulation for a ruin-repair neighborhood search process. It uses a Node-Pair Removal
2086   decoder in the ruin stage and a Node-Pair Reinsertion decoder in the repair stage, allowing efficient
2087   operation on pickup-delivery node pairs. However, more complex decoders increase computational
2088   costs in the policy network, requiring a balance between encoders and decoders. To address this,
2089   N2S introduces Synthesis Attention (Synth-Att), which learns a single set of embeddings and synthe-
2090   sizes attention scores from various node feature embeddings using a Multilayer Perceptron (MLP)
2091
       module. This promotes lightweight policy networks and enhances model expressiveness. The N2S
       encoder with the efficient Synth-Att represents a state-of-the-art design of improvement encoder,
2092
       which is adopted in the latest works Ma et al. (2022; 2024).
2093
2094   In RL4CO, N2S reuses the CPE encoding and the PPO-CL algorithm implemented in DACT. The
2095   efficient N2S encoder is also modularized and designed to be shared among other improvement
2096   methods to process the complex relationships between different feature embeddings.
2097
2098   C.4.3    N EU O PT (M A ET AL ., 2024)
2099
       A key bottleneck of improvement methods like DACT is their simplistic action space design, which
2100   typically uses smaller, fixed k values (2-opt or 3-opt) due to decoders struggling with larger, varying
2101   k. To address this, the latest improvement method introduces Neural k-Opt (NeuOpt), a flexible
2102   solver capable of handling any given k ≥ 2. NeuOpt employs an action factorization method to
2103   break down complex k-opt exchanges into a sequence of basis moves (S-move, I-move, E-move),
2104   with the number of I-moves determining the k value. This step-by-step construction allows the
2105   model to automatically determine a suitable k. Similar to variable neighborhood search, NeuOpt
       combines varying k values across search steps, balancing coarse-grained and fine-grained searches,


                                                         39
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2106
       which is crucial for optimal performance. NeuOpt also features a Recurrent Dual-Stream (RDS)
2107   decoder with recurrent networks and two decoding streams for contextual modeling and attention
2108   computation, effectively capturing the complex dependencies between removed and added edges.
2109
2110
       In RL4CO, NeuOpt is implemented by reusing the successful CPE and PPO-CL training modules
       from DACT, as well as the efficient encoder from N2S. This demonstrates the strength and versatility
2111
       of the RL4CO coding library, which allows for the easy integration of proven methodologies.
2112
2113
2114
       C.5     ACTIVE S EARCH M ETHODS
2115
       Active search methods are examples of transductive RL, in which an RL algorithm is run to finetune
2116   a pre-trained policy on specific test-time instances.
2117
2118
       C.5.1    ACTIVE S EARCH (AS) (B ELLO ET AL ., 2017)
2119
2120   In active search proposed by Bello et al. (2017), a model is fine-tuned to a single test instance. To
2121   this end, active search uses the same loss formulation as during the original training of the model.
2122   Over the course of the search process, the model’s performance on the single test instance improves,
2123   leading to the discovery of high-quality solutions. While active search is easy to implement, as the
2124
       search process closely follows the training process, it is often very slow since all model weights are
       adjusted for each test instance individually.
2125
2126
2127
       C.5.2    E FFICIENT ACTIVE S EARCH (EAS) (H OTTUNG ET AL ., 2022)
2128   Efficient active search (EAS), proposed by Hottung et al. (2022), builds upon the idea of active
2129   search and trains a model on a single instance at test time to enable a guided search. However, EAS
2130   only updates a subset of parameters during the search and allows most operations to be performed
2131   in parallel across a batch of different instances. This approach not only reduces computational costs
2132   but also results in a more stable fine-tuning process, leading to an overall improvement in solution
2133   quality.
2134
2135   D     B ENCHMARKING S ETUP
2136
2137   D.1     M ETRICS
2138
2139   D.1.1     G AP TO BKS
2140
2141   The Gap to Best Known Solution (BKS) is a commonly used metric to evaluate the performance
2142
       of optimization algorithms on benchmark instances. It measures the relative difference between the
       best solution found by the algorithm and the BKS for a given problem instance. Given a problem
2143
       instance i, let ai be the objective value of the best solution found by the algorithm, and let a∗i be the
2144
       objective value of the BKS for that instance. The Gap to BKS for the i-th instance is defined as:
2145
2146
                                                                          ai − a∗i
2147                                       Gap to BKSi = 100 ×                                                      (32)
2148
                                                                             a∗i
2149   The Gap to BKS is expressed as a percentage, with a value of 0% indicating that the algorithm
2150   has found a solution that matches the BKS. A positive Gap to BKS indicates that the algorithm’s
2151   solution is worse than the BKS, while a negative Gap to BKS (though less common) indicates that
2152   the algorithm has found a new best solution for the instance13 .
2153
2154   D.1.2     P RIMAL I NTEGRAL
2155
       The Primal Integral (PI) is a metric that evaluates the anytime performance of optimization algo-
2156
       rithms by capturing the trade-off between solution quality and computational time (Berthold, 2013;
2157
          13
2158
         PNote
             n
                  that when calculating the gap for a set of instances, one should       P do P  an average of gaps, i.e.
             i=1 Gap to BKSi , instead of calculating the gap of the average 100 ×          ai / a∗i , which might yield
       1
2159   n
       similar results in some settings but prone to error especially for certain distributions.


                                                              40
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2160
       Thyssens et al., 2023). It is defined as the area under the curve of the incumbent solution value
2161   plotted against time, normalized by the BKS value and the total time budget:
2162
2163                                    Pn
                                          i=1 ai−1 · (ti − ti−1 ) + an · (Tmax − tn )
2164                    P I = 100 ×                                                   −1                 (33)
2165
                                                         Tmax · a∗
2166   where Tmax is the total time budget, ai is the incumbent solution value at time ti , and a∗ is the
2167   best known solution value. A lower PI percentage indicates better anytime performance. The PI
2168   complements other metrics, such as the Gap to BKS, by providing insights into the temporal aspect
2169   of an algorithm’s performance, making it particularly useful for assessing anytime algorithms (Jesus
2170
       et al., 2020).
2171
2172   D.1.3    RUNTIME M EASUREMENT
2173
       Runtime normalization Comparing the run-time efficiency of different methods across various
2174
       hardware configurations can be challenging. In the RL4CO benchmark, we generally run the in-
2175   ference on a single machine; when this is not possible due to resource limitations, we employ the
2176   run-time normalization approach based on the PassMark hardware rating14 . This approach nor-
2177   malizes time budgets and run times during the evaluation process, allowing for a more equitable
2178   comparison of methods. We use the definition of Accorsi et al. (2022); Thyssens et al. (2023) in
2179   normalizing: the reference machine combines a single CPU thread and a single GPU, the PassMark
2180   score s for GPU-based methods is calculated as:
                                   1
2181                          s = (#CPU · CPU_Mark + #GPU · GPU_Mark)                               (34)
2182                               2
2183   To normalize the solution time from machine 1 to machine 2, we calculate t̃2 = t1 ss12 , where t1 is
2184   the solution time on machine 1, s1 is the PassMark score of machine 1, and s2 is the PassMark score
2185   of machine 2. Note that in the case of most classical solvers, the GPU_Mark is simply set to 0 due
2186   to them running on CPU.
2187
2188   Cross-solver comparisons Another aspect of NCO evaluation that has to be addressed is the fact
2189   that evaluation between classical and learned solvers is often done on different devices, namely on
2190   (single-threaded) CPUs and GPUs, respectively. Moreover, while multiple instances in NCO can
2191   usually be solved in a batch, this is not usually the case for classical solvers. A more correct way is
2192   to measure the per-instance solution time (which we do on large-scale NAR routing), which is more
2193   realistic for real-world applications. For other studies, we employ the standard procedure of NCO of
2194
       evaluating times on batches as done in the original methods, making sure to compare “apples with
       apples” (i.e., different NCO approaches are compared with the same settings). We note that while
2195
       RL4CO focuses on comparisons between NCO solvers and creating an open-source ecosystem for
2196
       this specific area, future studies (and possibly works in the RL4CO community) may also include
2197   comparisons with classical solvers under different conditions, which we recognize as an important
2198   research direction.
2199

2207
       amount of experiments and contributions, training was performed on a variety of hardware combina-
       tions, particularly University clusters. We found RL4CO to be robust and efficient across different
2208
       combinations of CPU, GPU, and software. Throughout the text, we may report the hardware setting
2209

2211   ization.

2218   in RL constructive methods for CO generally use attention for encoding states, FlashAttention has
2219   some boost on the performance (between 5% and 20% saved time depending on the problem size)
2220
       when training is subject to mixed-precision training, which we do for all experiments. During decod-
       ing, the FlashAttention routine is not called since, at the time of writing, it does not support maskings
2221
       other than causal; this could further boost performance compared to older implementations. Refer
2222
       to Appendix A.2 for additional details regarding notable software choices of our library, namely

       D.3.2    C HANGING P OLICY C OMPONENTS
2233
2234   We train the models evaluated in Table 3 using the same number of training instances as well as
2235   identical hyperparameters. Specifically, models are trained for 10 epochs on 2.000 training instances
2236   using the PPO algorithm with clip range ϵ = 0.2. The training dataset is split into batches of size
2237   100 to construct the replay buffer. For the PPO optimization we sample mini-batches of size 512
2238   from the replay buffer until it is empty and repeat this for R = 3 inner epochs. All models use
2239   an embedding dimension dh of 256. The number of encoder layersis set to L = 3 in each case.
2240
       Further, MatNet and the AM Pointer use H = 8 attention heads. The parameters of the models
       are updated using the Adam optimizer with learning rate 10−4 . Afterwards, the trained policies
2241
       are evaluated on 1.000 randomly generated test instances. The Hydra config files corresponding
2242
       to this experiment, which also implement the different model architectures, can be found in the

2249   optimizer with a learning rate of 10−4 and 0 weight decay. For POMO, we match the original im-
2250
       plementation details of weight decay as 10−6 . For POMO, the number of multistarts is the same as
       the number of possible initial locations in the environment (for instance, for TSP50, 50 starts are
2251
       considered). In the case of Sym-NCO, we use 10 as augmentation for the shared baseline; we match
2252
       the number of effective samples of AM-XL to the ones of Sym-NCO to demonstrate the differences
2253   between models.

       D.3.4     G ENERALIZATION : C ROSS -TASK AND C ROSS -D ISTRIBUTION
2346   In addition to training on uniformly distributed instances, as is standard for POMO Kwon et al.
2347   (2020), we further train POMO Kwon et al. (2020) on a mixture of multiple distributions (i.e., the
2348   exemplar distributions defined in (Bi et al., 2022)) and multiple VRP tasks (i.e., CVRP, OVRP,
2349   VRPL, VRPB, VRPTW, and OVRPTW, as defined in (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou et al., 2024; Berto
2350   et al., 2024)) with fixed problem size N = 50, termed as MDPOMO and MTPOMO, respectively.
2351   Note that all the models in Table 5 undergo training across 10,000 epochs, each with a batch size of
2352   512 and 10,000 training instances. The other training setups are consistent with the previous work
2353
       (Kwon et al., 2020). The whole training time is within one day. During inference, we evaluate their
       generalization performance on the benchmark datasets in CVRPLib Lima et al. (2014) using greedy
2354
       rollout with 8× instance augmentation and multiple start nodes following Kwon et al. (2020).

2358   The GLOP (Ye et al., 2024b) models’ global policy are trained on random instances of CVRP1K and
2359   CVRP2K, respectively. Both models are trained for 100 epochs, with each epoch comprising 1000
2360   instances. To accelerate the training process, random insertion is utilized as the sub-TSP solver.
2361
       For the experiment results presented in Table 6, we evaluate our implementation using the identical
2362   instances and setup as those utilized in Ye et al. (2024b). The AM revisers involved are directly
2363   adopted from Ye et al. (2024b). Table 14 reports the generalization performance of the CVRP2K

2372

2402   D.4.1    AUGMENTATIONS
2403
2404   In RL4CO, we consider as augmentations any transformation ψ that maps an instance x
2405
       into an instance x′ whose (optimal) solution should be the same or close to the original.
       Augmentations have been used in various domains, such as computer vision, Table 10: Dihedral
2406
       where, for example, labels are invariant to rotations. Similarly, in Euclidean transformations (Kwon
2407
       CO, one can apply the dihedral transformation of Table 10 to generate a et al., 2020).
2408   new instance whose solution is the same as the original one, composed of
2409   4 rotations and 2 flips for a total of ×8 transformation (which is the default         ψ(x, y)
2410   used in POMO-based models as Kwon et al. (2020); Liu et al. (2024a); Zhou
2411   et al. (2024). As introduced in Kim et al. (2022) , one may additionally use      (x, y)     (y, x)
       any angle θ to perform a symmetric transformation as follows:                    (x, 1-y)   (y, 1-x)
2412
                                                                                        (1-x, y) (1-y, x)
2413
                                        ′                                              (1-x, 1-y) (1-y, 1-x)
2414                                    x                  x cos θ −y sin θ
                                             = ψ(x, y) =
2415                                    y′                  x sin θ +y cos θ
2416
2417   where θ ∈ [0, 2π]. Interestingly, we found that, generally, the dihedral augmentation is worse in
2418   terms of sample efficiency compared to randomly augmenting by sampling a θ value. We note that
2419
       other augmentations are possible, including dilation (Bdeir et al., 2022) (i.e., rescaling) and possibly
       new ones such as jittering, which may have a broader application than Euclidean CO.
2420
2421
       D.4.2    S AMPLING
2422
2423   In most NCO approaches, sampling is performed by simply increasing the evaluation budget but
2424   without additional modifications that can be important for better performance. We include the fol-
2425   lowing techniques in RL4CO: 1) Sampling with Softmax Temperature, 2) Top-k Sampling and 3)
2426   Top-p Sampling, visualized in Fig. 16.
2427
2428   Sampling with Softmax Temperature Sampling with softmax temperature is a technique used to
2429   control the randomness of the sampling process. The temperature parameter τ is introduced to the
       softmax function, which converts the logits z into a probability distribution:

2447                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     pi = PN                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 (35)
