2448
2449   where pi is the probability of selecting the i-th action, zi is the corresponding logit, and N is the total
2450   number of actions. A higher temperature τ > 1 makes the distribution more uniform, increasing
2451   the chances of selecting less likely actions. Conversely, a lower temperature 0 < τ < 1 makes the
2452   distribution sharper, favoring the most likely actions.
2453
2454
       Top-k Sampling Top-k sampling is a method that restricts the sampling space to the k most likely
2455
       actions. Given the logits z, the top-k actions with the highest probabilities are selected, and the prob-
2456
       abilities of the remaining actions are set to zero. The probability distribution is then renormalized
2457   over the selected actions:
2458
2459                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     (
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             P exp(zi /τ )             if i ∈ Tk
2460                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              pi =        j∈T exp(zj /τ )
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  k                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              (36)
2461                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        0                          otherwise
2462
       where Tk is the set of indices corresponding to the top-k actions. Top-k sampling helps to eliminate
2463
       the possibility of generating low-probability actions, improving the quality and coherence of the
2464   generated output. We note that, however, in CO problems, it may not be as straightforward as
2465   in large language models to select the k parameter since neighborhoods and distributions are not
2466   homogeneous.
2467
2468
2469   Top-p Sampling Top-p sampling, also known as nucleus sampling, is an alternative to top-k sam-
2470   pling that dynamically adjusts the number of actions considered for sampling based on a probability
2471   threshold p (Holtzman et al., 2019). The actions are sorted by their probabilities in descending order,
2472   and the cumulative probability is calculated. The sampling space is then restricted to the smallest
2473
       set of actions whose cumulative probability exceeds the threshold p:
2474                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  
2475                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      i
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           X           
2476                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     Tp =         i:         pj ≤ p                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          (37)
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      
2477                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       j=1

2478   where Tp is the set of indices corresponding to the actions included in the top-p sampling. The
2479   probabilities of the actions in Tp are renormalized, while the probabilities of the remaining actions
2480   are set to zero:
2481
2482
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         (

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           46
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2484
       Top-p sampling provides a more dynamic way to control the diversity and quality of the generated
2485   output compared to top-k sampling. In CO, this is also a more structured way of performing training
2486   or evaluation since top-p sampling is agnostic of the number of nodes, unlike top-k sampling.

2489   E     A DDITIONAL E XPERIMENTS
2490
2491   E.1     M IND YOUR BASELINE : F URTHER I NSIGHTS
2492
       Benchmark Setup We focus on benchmarking the AR routing NCO solvers under controlled
2493
       settings, aiming to compare all benchmarked methods as closely as possible in terms of network
2494
       architectures and the number of training samples consumed.
2495
2496
       Models We evaluate the following NCO solvers: 1) AM (Kool et al., 2019a) with rollout baseline,
2497
       2) POMO (Kwon et al., 2020) with the shared baseline to train AM instead of the rollout baseline;
2498   we also use six MHA layers and InstanceNorm instead of BatchNorm according to the original
2499   implementation, 3) Sym-NCO (Kim et al., 2022) utilizes the symmetric baseline to train AM instead
2500   of the rollout baseline and the same encoder as POMO, 4) AM-XL is an AM model that adopts
2501   POMO-style MHA encoder, and trained on the same number of samples as POMO, with the goal of
2502   seeing whether training for longer, as done in POMO, can significantly improve the results 5) A2C,
2503   i.e. AM trained with Advantage Actor-Critic (A2C), 6) AM-PPO trained via the Proximal Policy
2504   Optimization (PPO, Schulman et al. (2017)) algorithm and finally 7) Polynet (Hottung et al., 2024)
2505   with shared baseline and setting K = n.
2506   For fairness of comparison, we try to match the number of training steps to be the same and adjust
2507   the batch size accordingly. Specifically, we train models for 100 epochs as in Kool et al. (2019a)
2508   using the Adam optimizer (Kingma and Ba, 2014) with an initial learning rate (LR) of 0.001 with
2509   a decay factor of 0.1 after the 80th and 95th epochs18 . We evaluate the trained solvers using the
2510   schemes shown in Fig. 15.
2511
2512   E.1.1    M AIN I N - DISTRIBUTION R ESULTS
2513
       We first measure the performances of NCO solvers on the same dataset distribution on which they
2514
       are trained. We first observe that, counter to the commonly known trends that AM < POMO < Sym-
2515
       NCO, the trends can change to decoding schemes and targeting CO problems. Especially when
2516   the solver decodes the solutions with Augmentation or Greedy Multistart + Augmentation, the per-
2517   formance differences among the benchmarked solvers on TSP and CVRP become less significant.
2518   Surprisingly, PolyNet performs well even in the greedy one-shot setting, despite its primary focus
2519   on generating diverse solutions. For decoding schemes that generate multiple solutions, PolyNet
2520   demonstrates strong performance across various problems. Particularly for decoding schemes with-
2521   out multistarts, PolyNet benefits significantly from its inherent diversity mechanism
2522   We note that the original implementation of POMO 19 is not directly applicable to OP, PCTSP, and
2523   PDP. Adapting it to solve new problems is not straightforward due to the coupling between envi-
2524   ronment and policy implementations. However, owing to the flexibility of RL4CO, we successfully
2525   implemented POMO for OP and PCTSP. Our results indicate that POMO underperforms in OP and
2526   PCTSP; unlike TSP, CVRP, and PDP, where all nodes need to be visited, OP and PCTSP are not
2527   constrained to visit all nodes. Due to such differences, POMO’s visiting all nodes strategy may not
2528   work as an effective inductive bias. Further, we benchmark the NCO solvers for PDP, which was
2529   not originally supported natively by each of the benchmarked solvers. We apply the environment
2530
       embeddings and the Heterogeneous Attention Encoder from HAM (Li et al., 2021) to the NCO mod-
       els for encoding pickup and delivery pairs, further emphasizing RL4CO’s flexibility. We observe
2531
       that AM-XL, which employs the same RL algorithm as AM but features the encoder architecture of
2532
       POMO and is trained with an equivalent number of samples, yields performance comparable to NCO
2533   solvers using more sophisticated baselines. This suggests that careful controls on architecture and
2534   the number of training samples are required when evaluating NCO solvers. We also re-implemented
2535
         18
2536        We find that simple learning rate scheduling with MultiStepLinear can improve performance i.e.,
2537   compared to the original AM implementation.
         19
            https://github.com/yd-kwon/POMO

2578   E.1.2       D ECODING S CHEMES C OMPARISON
2579
2580   During inference, investing more computational resources (i.e., sampling more), the trained NCO
2581   solver can discover improved solutions. We examine the performance gains achieved with varying
2582   numbers of samples. As shown in Fig. 17, the Augmentation decoding scheme achieves the Pareto
2583
       front with limited samples and, notably, generally outperforms other decoding schemes. We note
       that while sampling with a light decoder can be more efficient in terms of speed than sampling, this
2584
       may not be true for heavy-decoder (Luo et al., 2024a) or decoder-only models (Drakulic et al., 2023;
2585
       Luo et al., 2024b; Pirnay and Grimm, 2024), where decoding via greedy augmentations may help
2586   improve performance.
2587
2588   E.1.3       S AMPLE E FFICIENCY

2591   of reward evaluations). As shown in Fig. 18, we found that actor-critic methods (e.g., A2C and PPO)
       can exhibit efficacy in scenarios with limited training samples, as demonstrated by the TSP50/100

       sive, REINFORCE baselines that include additional reward function evaluations such as Greedy

2747   on the searched instances by adapting all the policy parameters and 2) Efficient Active Search (EAS):
2748   from (Hottung et al., 2022) which finetunes a subset of parameters (i.e., embeddings or new layers)

2750   We apply AS and EAS to POMO and Sym-NCO pre-trained on TSP and CVRP with 50 nodes to
2751   solve larger instances having N ∈ [200, 500, 1000] nodes. As shown in Table 13, solvers with search
2752   methods improve the solution quality. However, POMO generally shows better improvements over
2753   Sym-NCO. This suggests once more that the “overfitting” of sophisticated baselines can perform
       better in training distributions but eventually worse in different downstream tasks.

2826
       E.2     L EARNING H EURISTICS FOR A NT C OLONY O PTIMIZATION
2827
2828   E.2.1      E XPERIMENT S ETTINGS
2829
2830   We adhered to the hyperparameters specified in the original papers for DeepACO (Ye et al., 2023)
2831
       and GFACS (Kim et al., 2024) for GFlowNets training. We conducted two distinct benchmarks for
       ACO methods. The first benchmark evaluated the ability to solve the Traveling Salesman Problem
2832
       (TSP) at different scales: 200, 500, and 1000. We use the test instances provided by DeepACO20 .
2833
       The second benchmark assessed inference capability at various temperature values of τ in TSP with
2834   500 nodes. The temperature τ is a hyperparameter for the heatmap distribution of the heuristic
2835   matrix in ACO, where a low τ emphasizes exploitation and a high τ emphasizes exploration. For
2836   both experiments, the optimality gaps are calculated with respect to the average cost of solutions
2837   obtained using Concorde Applegate et al. (2023).
2838
2839   E.2.2      R ESULTS
2840
2841
       TSP Benchmark Table 15 shows the results for the first benchmark. In this benchmark, we ob-
       served that GFACS outperforms other baselines, and DeepACO surpasses ACO. These results are
2842
       consistent with their respective claims (Ye et al., 2023; Kim et al., 2024), providing evidence that
2843
       our benchmark is sufficiently valid. Notably, our algorithm also performed slightly faster than the
2844   original implementation, likely due to the batchified environment of RL4CO.
2845
2846   Performance Comparison for Different Heatmap Temperatures (τ ) Table 16 shows the re-
2847   sults for the second benchmark. This benchmark compared inference performance across different
2848   heatmap temperatures (τ ). We observed notable performance variation with changes in τ . This
2849   highlights the importance of inference and sampling strategies even after deep network training is
2850   completed. Additionally, GFACS produced more consistent results with different τ values. This
2851   provides empirical evidence of the robustness of GFACS, which is due to its ability to model a sam-
2852   pler capable of generating diverse and high-reward solutions. The modularization of RL4CO allows
2853   for a focused study on inference capabilities, enabling future researchers to contribute to this aspect
2854
       using the RL4CO pipeline.
2855
2856   E.3     L EARNING TO S CHEDULE
2857
       Compared to routing problems, scheduling problems have not been extensively studied by the NCO
2858
       community. On the one hand side, NCO methods for scheduling are harder to benchmark due to
2859   the absence of well-performing heuristics like the LKH algorithm for the TSP. On the other hand,
2860   scheduling problems involve more complex graph representations like disjunctive graphs Zhang
2861
         20
              https://github.com/henry-yeh/DeepACO


                                                                         53
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2862
       et al. (2020), bipartite graphs Kwon et al. (2021), or heterogeneous graphs Song et al. (2022), making
2863   it harder to encode the problem. With RL4CO, we aim to mitigate these entry barriers for NCO
2864   researchers by providing established solution methods along with the environments. Further, by
2865   being modular by design, RL4CO allows for quick evaluation of different learning algorithms and
2866   network architectures, which can already lead to substantial improvements of the solution quality,
2867   as demonstrated in the example of the FJSSP in Table 3. Lastly, by providing benchmark instances
2868   like Taillard Taillard (1993) and easy ways of initializing the environments with external benchmark
2869   files, we facilitate the comparison of models with existing methods. The following chapter describes
2870   established DRL models for scheduling problems as well as their performance on synthetic and
2871
       benchmark datasets.
2872
2873   E.3.1     JSSP
2874
       Models To solve the JSSP using DRL methods, we implement the L2D model described in Ap-
2875
       pendix C.2.7 in RL4CO. To train the encoder-decoder policy, we use the same Proximal Policy
2876   Optimization (PPO) algorithm as Zhang et al. (2020). In contrast to most other work in the NCO
2877   domain, L2D uses a (dense) stepwise reward function rather than a sparse episodic reward, which is
2878   observed only after a complete solution is obtained. This reward determines the change in the lower
2879   bound of the makespan given the partial schedule. Due to the dense nature of the reward, the PPO
2880   algorithm for the scheduling problems evaluates actions on a stepwise basis, whereas environments
2881   with an episodic reward are evaluated based on a full rollout. We compare these methods and discuss
2882   the different implementations in Appendix E.3.4.
2883   Further, we demonstrate RL4CO’s ability to effortlessly implement a state-of-the-art solver for JSSP
2884   instances by exchanging the GCN encoder used by Zhang et al. (2020) with the MatNet encoder
2885   Kwon et al. (2021) described in Appendix C.2.11. Furthermore, the greedy decoding scheme of
2886   Zhang et al. (2020) is replaced by N = 100 random samples, of which the best is selected.
2887
2888   Reproduction and Improvement of Original Results We demonstrate RL4CO’s capability of
2889   learning dispatching rules for the JSSP by training and validating the L2D model of Zhang et al.
2890   (2020) and our version of L2D with the MatNet encoder on synthetic data. We report the perfor-
2891   mance achieved with RL4CO together with the baselines the authors of the original papers used, as
2892   well as the solutions obtained via the CP-Sat solver Google OR-Tools. The baselines are a set of
2893
       selected PDRs that have a high practical relevance, namely Most Work Remaining (MWKR) and
       Most Operations Remaining (MOR).
2894
2895
       Table 17: Comparison of RL4CO with L2D Zhang et al. (2020) and other baselines on the JSSP. For OR-Tools,
2896   the fraction of instances solved optimally is reported in parentheses.
2897
2898                                                 PDRs                  L2D                      RL4CO
2899
          Size     Metric      OR-Tools
                                              MWKR        MOR       Zhang et al. (2020)    GCN       MatNet (×128)
2900
2901               Obj.      487.75 (100%)     656.96     630.19          574.09          569.53         515.11
         6×6
                   Gap             -           34.6%      29.2%           17.7%           16.8%           5.6%
2902
2903               Obj.      808.32 (100%)    1151.41    1101.08          988.58          972.35         865.78
        10 × 10
2904               Gap             -           42.6%      36.5%           22.3%           20.3%           7.1%
2905               Obj.      1187.06 (99%)    1812.13    1693.33         1504.79          1492.94       1318.25
        15 × 15
2906               Gap             -           52.6%      42.6%           26.7%            25.7%         11.0%
2907               Obj.      1555.79 (4%)     2469.19    2263.68         2007.76          1992.36       1847.33
        20 × 20
2908               Gap             -           58.6%      45.5%           29.0%            28.1%         18.7%
2909
2910   The results are listed in Table 17. RL4CO’s implementation of L2D manages to outperform the
2911   original implementation on all instance types, even when using the same model architecture, learning
2912   algorithm, and hyperparameters. The reason is that RL4CO uses an improved implementation of the
2913   environment. In the implementation of Zhang et al. (2020) the state of the environment does not
2914   contain a time dimension. Instead, the environment schedules the selected operation at the earliest
2915   feasible start time, given the current schedule. Here, we use the environment proposed by Tassel
       et al. (2021), where the environment transitions through distinct time steps t = 0, 1, ...T . In this


                                                          54
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


2916
       case, the start time of a selected operation is set to the time step at which it was selected, leading to
2917   a more natural form of credit assignment.
2918
2919   Using the MatNet encoder instead of the GCN and employing a decoding scheme based on multiple
2920
       random rollouts further reduces the makespan by a large margin. One instances of size 6 × 6, the
       gap to the optimal solutions was reduced by 11 percentage points to 5.6%, which corresponds to a
2921
       third of the gap realized with the GCN encoder.
2922
2923
2924   Taillard Benchmark and out-of-distribution performance With RL4CO, we also provide the
2925
       possibility to test models against established benchmarks. For the JSSP, a well-recognized bench-
       mark is that of Taillard (1993), which is also used by Zhang et al. (2020) to validate their model.
2926

2929   formance to demonstrate the model’s generalization ability. Similar to the synthetic test instances,
2930   our RL4CO implementation paired with the MatNet encoder manages to outperform the original
2931   L2D by large margins on all instances of the Taillard benchmark dataset, even when evaluating it on
2932   out-of-distribution instances.

2954
       Model To solve the FJSSP using DRL methods, we implement the HGNN model described in
2955
       Appendix C.2.10 in RL4CO and train it with the same PPO algorithm as L2D. Besides HGNN we
2956
       also implement a second model which exchanges the encoder of HGNN with the MatNet encoder.
2957
2958
2959   Reproduction and Improvement of Original Results We compare the results obtained via
2960
       RL4CO with those reported by Song et al. (2022) and the baseline used by them. Also, Song et al.
       (2022) use MWKR and MOR to benchmark their model as well as the OR-Tools solver. The results,
2961
       which are obtained on a test set comprising of 100 randomly generated instances, are listed below
2962
       in Table 19.
2963
2964   Similar to the JSSP, the HGNN implemented in RL4CO achieves better results than the original im-
2965   plementation, although both implementations use the same definition of the environment. However,
2966
       in RL4CO, we use instance normalization Ulyanov et al. (2016) on the input variables as well as
       between consecutive HGNN layers, which we found to drastically stabilize the training process.
2967
2968   Again, we were able to enhance the quality of the solution further by simply exchanging the encoder
2969   with MatNet. Especially on the larger instances, the increased model complexity translates into
       much better model performance, with the solutions even surpassing OR-Tools on 20 × 10 instances.

       models trained with RL4CO on FJSSP 20 × 10 instances, by evaluating them on smaller (20 × 5
2988   & 15 × 10) and larger (30 × 10 & 40 × 10) instances. The results in Table 20 indicate that both
2989   HGNN and MatNet manage to generalize well to problems of different sizes. Despite being trained
2990   on smaller instances, the HGNN manages to close the performance gap when evaluated on larger
2991   instances, with gaps being as small as 3.7% for FJSSP 40 × 10 instances. And on FJSSP 20 × 5
2992   instances, the average makespan increases by only 1.56 (0.8%) when using the model trained on
2993   FJSSP 20 × 10 instead of 20 × 5 instances. Again, the MatNet model shows superior perfor-
2994   mance compared to the other baselines and surpasses even the results obtained by OR-Tools on the
2995   larger instances. The within-distribution performance of MatNet, therefore, also translates to out-of-
2996
       distribution instances, indicating that the complexity of the model results in a better generalization
       ability.

3014   E.3.3      FFSP
3015   MatNet To solve the FFSP using DRL, RL4CO implements the policy network described by
3016   Kwon et al. (2021). It uses separate policy networks for each stage of the FFSP. Each of the stage
3017   networks employs the MatNet encoder described in Appendix C.2.11, which generates embeddings
3018   for jobs and machines using the processing times of the job-machine pairs of the respective stage.
3019   The decoder of the attention model Kool et al. (2019a) then utilizes the machine embeddings of
3020   the respective stage as query and the job embeddings as keys and values to compute the probability
3021   distribution over jobs.
3022
3023   Results We use the same three instance types described by Kwon et al. (2021) to evaluate our
       implementations of the FFSP environment and the policy network. The instances only differ in


                                                              56
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3024
       the number of jobs, which are set to 20, 50, and 100. We assume that there are S = 3 stages,
3025   and each stage has M = 4 machines. In the kth stage, the processing time of the job j on the
3026   machine m is given by pjmk . Therefore, an instance of the problem is defined by three matrices
3027   (P1 , P2 , and P3 ), specifying the processing time for each job-machine combination in that stage.
3028   We report the results obtained by RL4CO and compare them to those obtained by Kwon et al.
3029   (2021) in Table 21. Other benchmarks used are the exact solver CPLEX (for which results can
3030   only be obtained for FFSP20 instances), the Shortest Job First (SJF) dispatching rule, as well as the
3031   evolutionary algorithms Particle Swarm Optimization (PSO), and Genetic Algorithm (GA). One can
3032   see that, using RL4CO, we are able to reproduce the results from the original paper.
3033

3035   respect to the best known results.

3047   E.3.4    D ENSE AND E PISODIC R EWARDS
3048
       We additionally compare dense and episodic rewards for the TSP and FJSSP environments, with
3049
       similar training settings as in other experiments, except for the different reward functions.
3050
3051   Here, we compare the performance of the HGNN (Song et al., 2022) in solving the FJSSP and AM
3052   (Kool et al., 2019a) in solving the TSP when trained using a stepwise vs. an episodic reward. The
3053
       results in Table 22 show that evaluating the FJSSP in a stepwise manner and stepwise re-encoding
       the current state significantly outperforms a policy based on a single, episodic reward. This is
3054
       reasonable since the state of the FJSSP has many dynamic elements, and a policy that relies on a
3055
       single encoder step may not fully grasp the problem dynamics. On the other hand, stepwise rewards
3056   for the TSP (AM model trained with POMO with the settings as Kwon et al. (2020)) do not work
3057   well, and interestingly, performance approaches roughly that of the nearest insertion algorithms.
3058   Different CO problems react to the same learning setup, which again underpins the importance of a
3059   unified framework where different algorithms are implemented and are easily exchangeable.
3060
3061           Table 22: Comparison of dense (i.e. stepwise) and episodic rewards for the TSP and the FJSSP

                                          20      50      100        10 × 5    20 × 5    15 × 10

3074   Policy Gradients, an “alias” of the REINFORCE algorithm) with Critic baseline, DF(PG,Rollout):
3075   REINFORCE with Rollout baseline as well as PPO. All experiments are run with the same hyper-
3076   parameters as the other experiments except for the batch size set to 64, the maximum number of
3077   samples set to 10, 000, and a total of only 10 epochs due to the nature of the benchmark sample
       efficiency.

3119   E.4.2              G ENERALIZATION TO D IFFERENT N UMBER OF C OMPONENTS
3120
3121   In hardware design, the number of components is one major contribution to cost; ideally, one would
3122   want to use the least number of components possible with the best performance. In the DPP, in-
3123   creasing the number of decaps generally improves the performance at a greater cost, hence Pareto-
3124
       efficient models are essential to identify. Fig. 25 shows the performance of DF models trained on
       20 decaps against the baselines. DF models PPO and PG-rollout can successfully generalize and are
3125
       also Pareto-efficient with fewer decaps, important in practice for cost and material saving.

3152   et al. (2021) (TSP50), N2S Ma et al. (2022) (PDP50), and NeuOpt Ma et al. (2024) (PDP50)—using

3154   ically, training and testing times (T = 1, 000) are faster, and peak memory usage is lower. This
3155

       nary variable to store all state information, and the incorporation of efficient libraries like PyTorch
3156
       Lightning and TorchRL. These enhancements demonstrate RL4CO’s superior efficiency and ease of
3157
       implementation.

       E.5.2                     D ISCUSSION
3174   As shown in Fig. 26, bootstrapping improvement with constructive methods can greatly improve
3175   the performance, especially in terms of the Primal Integral (PI, Appendix D.1.2). While in TSP
3176   bootstrapping is consistently better than simply improving with default solutions (i.e. lower final gap
3177   to BKS as well as PI), we note that in PDP with N2S, improving starting from a random initialization
3178   can yield better performance in terms of gap. However, the PI reveals that while N2S from random
3179   init achieves a value of 5.580, N2S from HAM construction initialization achieves a much better
3180   2.234, indicating a much better early convergence speed and Pareto front.
3181   We additionally offer some clues on how to improve such performance. Firstly, we simply initialized
3182   from a greedy solution, while more complex inference strategies may offer a significant boost. Fur-
3183   thermore, the trained model as per the setting in Appendix D.3.3 could be further trained and obtain
3184   better performance. Importantly, we believe that end-to-end construction & improvement, in which
3185   both a constructive and improvement method are trained together, could ultimately outperform a
       separate training and achieve the best of both worlds.

3201   E.6     G RAPH P ROBLEMS : FACILITY L OCATION P ROBLEM (FLP) AND M AXIMUM C OVERAGE
3202           P ROBLEM (MCP)
3203
       Here, we present the experimental results and the corresponding discussions on the two CO problems
3204   on graphs: the Facility Location Problem (FLP; see Appendix B.4.1) and the Maximum Coverage
3205   Problem (MCP; see Appendix B.4.2).
3206
3207   E.6.1    E XPERIMENTAL SETTINGS
3208
3209   Baseline methods We consider two simple baselines: uniform random (UR) and deterministic
3210   greedy (DG), where UR chooses k locations uniformly at random and DG chooses k locations one
3211
       by one in a greedy manner. We also apply two MIP solvers, Gurobi (Gurobi Optimization, 2021)
       and SCIP (Bestuzheva et al., 2021), to obtain the optimal solutions.
3212
3213
       Benchmark methods We benchmark with the attention model (AM) with different embedding
3214
       models (i.e., encoders) and different RL baselines. For FLP, the considered embedding models are:
3215
       the multilayer perception (MLP), the graph convolutional network (GCN) (Kipf and Welling, 2017),
3216   and the graph attention network (Velickovic et al., 2017; Brody et al., 2019). For MCP, since the
3217   problem instances are formulated on bipartite graphs, the considered embedding models are: the
3218   multilayer perception (MLP), the GraphSAGE model (Hamilton et al., 2017) (in short “SAGE”),
3219   and the generalized GCN model (Li et al., 2020a) (in short “GEN”). The considered RL baselines
3220   are: Rollout, Mean, Exponential, and Critic. All the models are trained in 100 epochs. The learning
3221   rate is 1e − 5 for FLP and 1e − 4 for MCP. In each epoch, 100, 000 training data are used with batch
3222   size 1, 000. For the decoding strategies, we consider sampling (with 64 independent samples) and
3223   greedy. For sampling (and UR), we report both the “best” performance among the 64 independent
3224
       samples and the “mean” (i.e., average) performance over the 64 independent samples.
3225
3226
       Test-time active search We apply three variants of active search at test time: the original active
       search (AS) proposed by Bello et al. (2017), efficient active search (EAS) proposed by Hottung
3227
       et al. (2022) with two variants: EAS-Emb that finetunes embeddings and EAS-Lay that finetunes
3228
       new layers. We run all the active search variants for 100 iterations.
3229
3230   E.6.2    B ENCHMARK R ESULTS
3231
3232   Main benchmark Table 25 shows the main numerical results when the methods are trained and
3233   tested to choose k = 10 locations on instances with n = 100 locations. Table 26 shows the main
3234   numerical results when the methods are trained and tested to choose k = 10 sets on instances with
3235   n = 100 sets and m = 200 items in total. Each item has a random weight between 1 and 10, and
3236
       the number of items in each set is randomly sampled between 5 and 15. The reported results are
       averaged over 1, 000 randomly generated test instances. We also report the average gap between the
3237
       performance for each setting and the optimum by solvers as described in Appendix D.1.1.
3238
3239   Here we use absolute values since we minimize the total distance for FLP while maximizing the
       total weights for MCP. When using absolute values, it is consistent that smaller gaps correspond to

3274
3275   better performance. The performance of RL methods with sampling is consistently better than the
3276   two baselines, uniform random (UR) and deterministic greedy (DG), showing their effectiveness on
3277
       those two problems.
3278
3279   Effect of the encoder Overall, the performance of different encoders is similar. For FLP, we can
3280   observe GCN’s marginal superiority (except when we use Critic as the RL baseline). For MCP, the
3281   best encoders for different RL baselines are different, but MLP’s performance is the overall best.
3282
3283   Effect of the RL baseline For FLP, for the four considered RL baselines (Rollout, Mean, Expo-
3284   nential, Critic), Rollout is consistently better than the other three. For MCP, the differences in the
3285   performance of different RL baselines are not significant.
3286
3287
       Effect of active search Active search significantly improves performance in almost all cases. For
3288   FLP, interestingly, Rollout achieves the best overall performance without active search, but Rollout
3289   underforms in many cases with test-time active search. Notably, the performance of the original
3290   active search (AS) is less stable than the two variants of efficient active search (EAS), especially for
3291   MCP. In our understanding, AS was originally designed for routing problems and uses multi-start
3292   sampling with distinct initial action (i.e., the first location/set to choose). Such a strategy is useful
3293   for routing problems due to symmetry but is less useful for problems without symmetry, such as
       FLP and MCP.

3327
3328   Test-time sampling techniques We also consider other test-time sampling techniques: top-p sam-
3329   pling (Holtzman et al., 2019) and different sampling temperatures. Top-p sampling discards actions
3330   with low probabilities, and top-p sampling with lower p values discards more low-probability ac-
3331   tions. For sampling temperatures, higher temperatures give more uniform sampling. The considered
3332   p values are: 0.5, 0.6, 0.7, 0.8, 0.9, 0.95, 0.99, 1.0. The sampling temperatures considered are 0.01,
3333   0.03, 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.8, 0.9, 1.0, 1.1, 1.2, 1.5, and 2.0. Fig. 27 show the heatmaps for each
3334   combination of encoder and RL baseline, for FLP and MCP. In each subplot, the x-axis represents
3335   the value of p in top-p sampling, and the y-axis represents the sampling temperature. For each com-
3336
       bination, the best performance is marked with a red star. For FLP, the best performance is usually
       achieved with a proper (i.e., neither too high nor too low) level of randomness. As the p value of
3337
       top-p sampling increases, the best sampling temperature decreases. Recall that both increasing the p
3338
       value and increasing the sampling temperature would increase the randomness in sampling. Overall,
3339   compared to other RL baselines, Rollout needs a higher level of randomness to perform best. For
3340   MCP, the best performance is usually achieved without top-p sampling and with a high sampling
3341   temperature, i.e., without high randomness in the sampling space.

       to the optimum.

3576   E.7.2             F LASH ATTENTION
3577
3578   Given that the Attention operator is used on several occasions, especially in autoregressive models,
3579   there is a need to support fast and efficient software routines that can compute this ubiquitous op-
3580
       eration. In RL4CO, we natively support FlashAttention (Dao et al., 2022; Dao, 2023) from both
       PyTorch 2.0+ and the original FlashAttention repository 21 , to which we also made some minor
3581
       contributions when we found bugs.

3598   Figure 29: Running time of the graph attention encoder from the Attention Model, equipped with a standard
3599   attention layer, FlashAttention1, and FlashAttention2, across different problem sizes for both (a) the TSP and

3601
3602
       As shown in Fig. 29, different implementations can make a difference, especially with large problem
       sizes. It should be noted that while more scalable, FlashAttention at the moment is restricted to no
3603
       or causal masks only. Therefore, usage in the masked attention decoding scheme is not possible
3604
       at the moment, although it could be even more impactful due to the auto-regressive nature of our
3605   encoder-decoder scheme. Recent works as Pagliardini et al. (2023) may be useful in extending
3606   FlashAttention to other masking patterns. We note that masking should, in principle, be even faster
3607   than un-masked attention, given that operations can be skipped in a per-block manner.
3608
3609   E.7.3             E FFICIENT M EMORY H ANDLING IN E NVIRONMENTS
3610
3611   When dealing with RL problems, there is usually a tradeoff between memory and speed. This
3612
       happens because environments are parallelized using multiple processes or threads, the policy net-
       work is replicated to each environment, or observations incoming from each environment need
3613
       to be gathered, sent to the policy network, and then the output action scattered back to the
3614
       representative environment. In the first case, network duplication causes large memory con-
3615   sumption; in the second case, communication between processes slows down. In RL4CO, we
3616   solve the problem by using batched environments, i.e., every environment is responsible not
3617
         21
               Available at https://github.com/Dao-AILab/flash-attention.


                                                                        67
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


3618
       for a single instance of a problem but a batch of instances at the same time. By doing so,
3619   the policy can live in the same process of the environment, in the same device, and receive
3620   and send batched data without any communication overhead or additional memory consumption.
3621   To further improve performances, we rewrite
3622   a core component of the TorchRL environ- Table 30: Comparison of training time in seconds for
3623   ment, namely the step method of the TorchRL one epoch with RL4CO and TorchRL step method.
3624   base environment. The original step method
3625   performs some checks that, while useful for              Configuration         Step method
3626   generic environments, can be omitted for             Environment Nodes RL4CO TorchRL
3627
       RL4CO ones. It also duplicates the informa-                         50         46.3       49.6
       tion in the output TensorDict by return-             TSP            100       102.9      108.6
3628
       ing both the previous and the new state. In                         200       284.9      302.2
3629
       RL4CO, the previous state is always redun-                          50         72.9       73.4
3630   dant, hence our step method does not keep it,        CVRP           100       147.3      154.3
3631   reducing the memory consumption. We can see                         200       371.7      406.4
3632   in Table 30 that using RL4CO step method has
3633   a great benefit in terms of speed, especially for high-dimensional environments. The results are
3634   collected for the TSP and CVRP environment during one epoch of training for a dataset of size
3635   100000. The table shows the difference in training time and peak allocated memory for the training
3636   when the environment uses the TorchRL step method and the RL4CO step method. The peak al-
3637   located memory is computed using the torch.cuda.max_memory_allocated method from
3638
       PyTorch, and experiments are run on a Tesla V100 DGX 32GB.
3639
3640   E.8     T OWARDS F OUNDATION M ODELS
3641
       Motivation Although learning to solve VRPs has gained significant attention, previous methods
3642
       are only structured and trained independently on a specific problem, making them less generic and
3643
       practical. Inspired by the recent success of foundation models in the language and vision domains,
3644   some works started to build foundation models for VRPs (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou et al., 2024;
3645   Berto et al., 2024), aiming to solve a wide spectrum of problem variants using a single model.
3646   The main idea is to train a (large) model on diverse VRPs, which can be represented by a unified
3647   template. Typically, VRPs share several common attributes. For example, CVRP and VRPTW
3648   share the capacity attribute while only differing in the time window attribute. Therefore, a simple
3649   template could be a union set of attributes that exist in all VRP variants. By training on diverse
3650   VRP variants leveraging this unified representation, the foundation VRP model has the potential to
3651   efficiently and effectively solve any variant, making it a favorable choice versus traditional solvers
3652
       (e.g., OR-Tools (Perron and Furnon, 2023)) in the future.
3653
3654
       E.8.1    E XPERIMENTAL S ETTING
3655   For traditional solvers, we use HGS-PyVRP (Wouda et al., 2024), an open-source VRP solver
3656   based on the state-of-the-art HGS-CVRP (Vidal, 2022), and Google’s OR-Tools (Perron and Furnon,
3657   2023), an open-source solver based on constraint programming for complex optimization problems,
3658   to solve all VRP variants considered in this study. Both baseline methods solve each instance on
3659   a single CPU core with a time limit of 10 and 20 seconds for instances with 50 and 100 nodes,
3660   respectively. We parallelize traditional solvers across 16 CPU cores as in (Kool et al., 2019a). For
3661   neural solvers, we mostly follow the training setups from previous works (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou
3662   et al., 2024; Berto et al., 2024). In specific, the model is trained over 300 epochs, with each epoch
3663
       containing 100,000 instances generated on the fly. The Adam optimizer is used with a learning rate
       of 3e − 4, a weight decay of 1e − 6, and a batch size of 256. The learning rate decays by 10 at 270
3664
       and 295 epochs. Note that different from Liu et al. (2024a); Zhou et al. (2024), we allow various
3665
       problem variants to be trained in each batch training following Berto et al. (2024). We consider 16
3666   VRP variants as shown in Table 8, including the constraints of capacity, time window, backhaul,
3667   open route, and duration limit. The training variants include CVRP, OVRP, VRPL, VRPB, VRPTW,
3668   and OVRPTW. During inference, we use greedy rollout with x8 instance augmentation following
3669   Kwon et al. (2020). We report the average results (i.e., objective values and gaps) over the test
3670   dataset that contains 1,000 instances, and the total time to solve the entire test dataset. The gaps
3671   are computed with respect to the results of HGS-PyVRP. All neural solvers are implemented using
       RL4CO.

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

3710   E.8.2           E MPIRICAL R ESULTS
3711
3712   We show the comprehensive evaluation results and validation curves in Table 31 and Fig. 30, re-
3713
       spectively. The conclusions are consistent with previous studies (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou et al.,
       2024; Berto et al., 2024) that 1) the foundation VRP solvers exhibit remarkable zero-shot gener-
3714
       alization performance, even only trained on several VRPs with simple constraints; 2) conditional
3715
       computation (e.g., mixture-of-experts (Jacobs et al., 1991; Shazeer et al., 2017)) can greatly en-
3716   hance the model capacity without a proportional increase in computation. In Table 32, we further
3717   show the performance on CVRPLIB (Lima et al., 2014), which is a real-world benchmark dataset in-
3718   cluding instances with diverse distributions. We empirically observe that training on multiple VRPs
3719   can significantly improve the out-of-distribution generalization performance of neural VRP solvers,
3720   demonstrating the great promise of developing foundation models in VRPs.
3721
3722   E.8.3           D ISCUSSION
3723
3724   Foundation models, a class of large-scale deep learning models pre-trained on extensive datasets
3725   of diverse tasks, have recently revolutionized the fields of language and vision domains. They can
       generate text, translate languages, summarize content, and more, all without task-specific training.

       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

3744                                    Step                                           Step                                            Step
3745
                                    Figure 30: The validation curves of foundation models on N = 100.
3746
3747
3748   This versatility makes them incredibly useful across various applications, from chatbots to academic
3749   research. Aiming for a more powerful and general solver, recent studies explore the possibility of
3750   pretraining a large model on a huge amount of optimization tasks. The long-term goal is to develop
3751   a foundation model for VRPs (or more broadly COPs), which can efficiently solve any problem
3752
       variant, comparably or better to the conventional solvers with respect to the solution quality and in-
       ference speed. Despite the recent advancements of foundation VRP models (Liu et al., 2024a; Zhou
3753
       et al., 2024; Berto et al., 2024), there are many challenges that need to be addressed by the NCO
3754
       community, including but not limited to: 1) scaling: current autoregressive-based models are chal-
3755   lenging to scale to the parameter levels of large language models (e.g., billions of parameters) due
3756   to the expensive training cost. RL-based training is data inefficient and converges slowly, whereas
3757   SL-based training requires a significant amount of optimal solutions, which are non-trivial to obtain
3758   for NP-hard problems. They also fail to be efficiently trained on large-scale instances; 2) perfor-
3759   mance: the empirical results are still far short of traditional solvers (e.g., OR-Tools). They may
3760   also suffer from generalization and robustness issues; 3) generality: the current problem formula-
3761   tion or template cannot solve novel problem variants in a zero-shot manner; 4) interpretability: the
3762   decision-making of foundation models is hard to explain.
3763   Moreover, there is another line of research leveraging the existing large language models (LLMs) to
3764   generate solutions (Yang et al., 2024; Liu et al., 2023; Iklassov et al., 2024) or algorithms (Romera-
3765   Paredes et al., 2024; Liu et al., 2024b; Ye et al., 2024a), yielding impressive results when integrated
3766   with problem-specific heuristics or general meta-heuristics. Some studies employ LLMs to investi-
3767   gate the interpretability of solvers (Kikuta et al., 2024), automate problem formulation or simplify
3768   the use of domain-specific tools (Xiao et al., 2024; AhmadiTeshnizi et al., 2024; Wasserkrug et al.,
3769
       2024) through text prompts. However, their performance is highly dependent on the utilized LLMs,
       and their outputs may be extremely sensitive to the designed prompts.
3770
3771   We view both as promising directions towards foundation models in combinatorial optimization. We
3772   call the attention from both the machine learning (ML) and operations research (OR) communities to
3773   advance the development of impactful foundational models and learning methods that are scalable,
3774   robust, generalizable, and interpretable across various optimization tasks in future work.
3775
3776   E.9          G ENERALIZATION OF T RAINING ON M ULTIPLE D ISTRIBUTIONS AND M ULTIPLE TASKS
3777
3778   Recent neural methods mostly train and test neural networks on the same task with instances of
3779   the same distribution and size, and hence suffer from inferior generalization performance. Some
       attempts have been made to alleviate the generalization issue, focusing on either distribution (Bi


                                                                                  70
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

3791
       et al., 2022; Jiang et al., 2022; Xin et al., 2022) or size (Son et al., 2023). More aligned to the
3792   diverse distribution and size settings in the benchmark dataset TSPLib and CVRPLib, Manchanda
3793   et al. (2023) and Zhou et al. (2023) consider generalization across both distribution and size in VRPs.
3794
3795
       However, these generalization methods adopt extra model architectures and training paradigms, re-
       sulting in additional computational burdens. As a more efficient alternative, we observe that diversi-
3796
       fied training datasets significantly improve generalization performance. Specifically, as indicated in
3797
       the prior works, training on mixed distributions (Bi et al., 2022) and mixed VRP variants (Liu et al.,
3798   2024a; Zhou et al., 2024; Berto et al., 2024) boosts the generalization capability. RL4CO, detailed
3799   in Appendix B.1.6, supports multiple VRP variants and the generation of diverse coordinate distri-
3800   butions, enabling straightforward experimental setups. The implementation specifics are outlined in
3801   Appendix D.3.4. Evaluation results on the CVRPLib Lima et al. (2014), summarized in Table 5 and
3802   fully detailed in Table 33, demonstrate that training across multiple distributions (i.e., MDPOMO)
3803   achieves better generalization on datasets of similar size to the training set, whereas training across
3804   multiple VRP tasks (i.e., MTPOMO) exhibits superior generalization across larger and more diverse
3805   distributions. This indicates that different VRP variants share foundational knowledge, and learning
3806
       from this diversity enhances generalization beyond conventional training on a single distribution,
       size, and task. These key findings highlight the necessity of developing foundational models across
3807
       diverse combinatorial optimization domains.
